Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large—from ioo up to ISO feet frontages, with long depths

is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.

and all in any way suffering from the above would derive

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 24, 1891.
Chaos.
Last week the league and covenant between
the New South Wales Labour-party and the
Parkes Government crumbled away, and
chaos supervened. The resolution of the
people’s delegates to squeeze certain demo-
cratic concessions out of the slippery bigot
who runs the New South Wales Foreign-
trade system was a risky one, for it involved
placing a certain amount of faith in Parkes,
and every separate individual who ever vet
put his faith in Parkes has been “left,” 'lt
also implied a firm conviction that the new
and inexperienced pffi-ty were a match for
the old politician in the matter of wiles and
dodges and strategy ; and Parkes has
devoted his whole career to learning dodges
—in fact, he has devoted himself so ex-
clusively to this one branch that he has
never had time to learn statesmanship, or
even political honesty—until, in his old age,
he is more devious and more unreliable than
a cart-load of serpents. As for Parkes’
share in the combination, it consisted in
promising everything under the sun that any
Labour-party could demand, and in so
arranging matters as to make it absolutely
certain that the Upper House would cancel all
his concessions, leaving him all the kudos of
being a vigorous democrat, together with all
the advantages of being a bigoted Tory, And
as Parkes needed the support both of the old
Foreign-trade-party and of the new Labour-
party to keep him in office, and as it was
essential that he should be a Tory to retain
the Foreign-trade vote and a Democrat to
hold the Labour vote, the advantages of thi
arrangement are sufficiently obvious.
# # «
The grim and ghastly jokes which, as can
now be seen, the old man passed upon the
Labour-party in the course of their connec-
tion were many, but the grimmest of them
all was perpetrated the other day when he
announced a “call” of the House for the
third reading of the Redistribution of Seats
and One-Man-One-Vote Bills. The “call”
was obviously useless, because, though it was
possible to compel every member who was
not disabled by illness to attend on the occa-
sion, there was no provision to make the
members remain when once they did attend
or to make them vote supposing they
remained ; and, therefore, in all probability
there would be no larger an attendance at
the actual division than there would have
been without that ceremony. It was useless
also because the third reading of any Bill is
almost always a mere formal matter ; because
the battle was already over, and the majority
in favour of the two measures was absolutely
certain. Moreover, the scheme was actually
injurious, because it was more likely to rally
up enemies than friends, for the friends
of the measure were mostly the youn»
and vigorous members whose attendance was
tolerably reliable in any case, and its
enemies were the old and crippled fossils
whom Parkes thus proposed to drag out of
their beds that they might create obstruc-
tion. Also, it involved the waste of three
precious weeks, and made it almost a
matter of certainty that both the Bills would
be left unfinished in the Upper House at the
end of the session. Parkes on this occasion
assumed the aspect of a toilworn patriot
who was dying at his oar in the desperate
effort to shove the democratic cause through
an exceedingly tight place, and apparently
the Labour-party failed to see that no such
effort was required, and that in any case he
was shoving in the wrong direction. It was
the sort of humour in which the ancient
politician delights. His inherent inability
to go straight under any possible circum-
stances makes this kind of joke particularly
refreshing to his soul.
* * *
But a few days later the Labour-party
furnished Parkes with another and an
equally biilliant opportunity through their
utter inability to understand the nature of
the situation. In the debate upon the Coal-
mines Regulation Bill, they succeeded in
inducing the spineless Sydney Smith to
accept a new clause by which miners were
prohibited, under a penalty, from working
more than seven hours and forty minutes
per diem, while on one Saturday in each
fortnight they were to be limited to six
hours labour, and on the other they were
not to labour at all. The one fixed principle
on which the labouring population of Aus-
tralia endeavours to travel is to limit the
hours of labour to eight per diem, and the
democracy desires to have this limit enforced
by law for various reasons. There is in
every community work enough under
equitable conditions for. the entire popu-
lation ; a nation of one million con-
sumers must be capable of furnishing
employment for one million producers if
the work is evenly apportioned, and when
an unemployed problem exists it can only be
because a certain section of the workers are
compelled each to do much more than one
man’s work in order to earn enough for
reasonable maintenance. It is estimated
that, under present circumstances, a uni-
versal eight hours limit for adults, with
restrictions against the use of child-labour,
will make the available work sufficient to go
round, and—until the improvement of
machinery or some other cause again deranges
the balance, and makes a further reduction
necessary—the eight hours standard is
generally accepted. Therefore it was the
business of any clear-sighted democratic
party to enforce this standard. The weary
slave who sits for 12 or 14 hours daily on the
box of an omnibus in the rain and wind and
blinding sunshine—the other slave who acts
as cook or waiter, often for 16 hours daily,
in the cheap restaurant where the leper’s
cabbage and the meat from the animal that
died a natural death are served up at six-
pence a head—the hard-driven “hand” on
the harbour steamer—the sailors, officers,
stewards, and the like on coasting vessels—
the hairdresser’s and tobacconist’s assistant
—the counter-jumper in the cheap and nasty
warehouse—the newspaper-reporter, whose
work is never finished by day or night—the
domestic “slavey”—the sweated dress-
maker, and a vast number of others are solid
evidences that the eight-hours system is as
yet only beginning even in Australia.
Nothing but drastic legislation can cope
with the evils of the gigantic sweating-
system which pervades the land in all
directions despite eight-hour demonstrations,
and similar advertisements.
» * «
The majority of these over-worked toilers,
too, are paid by fixed wages, and if their
hours were forcibly reduced by Act of Par-
liament, their remuneration, in most cases,
would remain the same. For the artibcial
superfluity of labourers would be imme-
diately diminished, and it is this same
artificial superfluity which keeps down the
pay of the hardest-worked members of the
community to a starvation limit, while their
hours of labour are kept up in an inverse
ratio. But most of these oppressed workers
have little organisation and no political in-
fluence, and either for this or some other
reason, no one—not even the Labour-party
—pays much attention to their grievances.
There was a strange and horrible fatuousness
in beginning the reform with the mining
men alone —the men with the great and solid
vote, whose hours are, in most cases, already
on or about the eight-hour limit; and when
it was proposed to reduce them even below
that limit, while leaving all the great army
of politically uninfluential twelve and four-
teen hours serfs to slide, the situation grew
still worse. And when it is remembered
that the miner is paid by results, so that his
own accepted principle was being violated
to reduce his wages—when he was rendered
liable to be fined if he worked the recognised
eight hours, which was another gratifying
feature to the subterranean toiler—and when,
in short, the whole reform was to be made
at the expense of the worker, and not at all
at the expense of the employer, the measure
assumed precisely the shape that Parkes
most desired. He was sick unto death of
the Labour-party ; his Government was in a
state of hopeless bankruptcy ; and when a
chance came to fall profitably, he fell. He
demanded the withdrawal of the obnoxi-
ous clause, and thereby he was enabled to
posture as the tri e and genuine friend of
the working classes, who came to grief
through defending them against (he tyranny
of their own overbearing representatives.
With much ingenuity and much skilful
management he induced the great majority
of his old allies of the Labour-party to vote
against him, that he might get away from
the wrath to come. Mr. Dirbs, the one far-
seeing man in the crowd—orthemau who went
right by accident—paired for Parkes —an act
for which that wily politician would probably
have brained him with much pleasure—and
had he been present in person he might
possibly have induced enough of his party to
go with him to block the old strategist’s
retreat Unfortunately a domestic calamity
compelled his absence, and Parkes success-
fully got away.
» # *
Parkes’ retirement is a picturesque act of
vengeance on the Protectionists and the
Labour-party, separately and conjointly. To
the former he is desirous of leaving his
gigantic deficiency, at a time when the old
loans are rapidly falling due, and new loans
are almost unobtainable, and when the un-
provided-for liabilities of the Works Depart-
ment alone total some £2,250,000. To the
latter he leaves a legacy of woe, for it is
almost certain that amid the confusion to
come the One Man-One-Yote Bill, the Seats
Redistribution Bill, the Coal-mines Bill, and
all the other unfinished measures of the
session will be hopelessly wrecked, and for
the rest of his life he will keep on explain-
ing how the vote of the Labour-party,
given in ignorance and blind con-
fusion, brought about the wreckage. The
occasion was about the only one
in all Parkes’s career when every
Democrat and every Protectionist in the
House should have voted for h’m as one
man. For it was necessary to make him
face his own shortage, brought about by his
own ruinous policy, and show to the country
the real consequences of the Foreign-trade
system, instead of allowing him to hand it
over—as he hopes to do—to his successors,
and to denounce it afterwards as an alto-
gether new shortage brought about by Pro-
tection. And it was equally necessary, if he
wished to wreck the One-man-one-vote Bill
and the other democratic measures before
the House, to make him wreck them bv his
own act, instead of giving him the oppor-
tunity to pose as the man who fell in defence
of a great reform because a Labour-party,
less democratic than himself, stood in the
way. Apparently Mr. Dirbs saw both these
points, and—whether by accident or design
his action was that of a profoundly
sagacious politician. Unfortunately his own
party declined to follow its leader and the
Labour-party had no leader to follow, and
the result was chaos.
* * *
There is no reason to believe that the
Labour-party knew the meaning of its own
action on this particular occasion. It simply
ran amok in the dark with an axe, and until
daylight came it didn’t even know what it
had smashed. It had no intention of block-
ing the whole of its own programme and un-
doing all that had been done with so much
toil merely for the sake of forcing the coal-
miner to work 20 minutes less per diem at his
own expense. There is no cause to believe that
it wanted to reduce the miner’s hours by Act
of Parliament simply because he is a great
political power, and that it left out thousands
of other toilers who are worked infinitely
longer and paid even worse than the miner,
simply because they are not any political
power worth speaking of. It had a general
impression that the one thing that ought to
be done for any labourer was to reduce his
toil, and it failed to see the utter folly of
trying to improve on the Eight Hours’
principle before that principle was itself
established. If the Party had demanded
that the eight-hours limit should be en-
forced for the miner, and for every other
labourer as well , male or female, voter or
non-voter, the people would have been with
it unanimously ; if it had introduced a Bill
to that effect it would have commanded suc-
cess. But instead of introducing its own
Bills, it always invited Parkes to do the
work fcr it, and then it had either to accept
his hideously impotent measures as they
stood or else to plunge about with incoherent
and chaotic amendments which were em-
blematic of its own chaotic condition. It
should have been its own Parkes from the
outset.
Close Up Your Ranks!
TO THE MEMBERS OF THE N.S.W. LABOUR
PARTY IN PARLIAMENT.
(Per favour of The Bulletin.,)
Brother Workers,—You occupy the most dig-
nified, the most potential, the most enviable of
positions. \on are not the ordinary represen-
tatives of the constituencies of a great Legisla-
ture—*you are not merely the custodians of
general public interests—you are not simply
the repositories of a tremendous public trust.
You are all these, but you share these repre-
sentative and those honourable functions with
a number of other men who cannot claim your
peculiar, your unique, distinction. You have
every prerogative which every member of a
British Parliament lias, but you possess others
altogether singular and exceptional. You are
the symbols of the Revolution—the exponents
of deep-seated wrong crying for redress—the
avant-couriers of the New Spirit—the expres-
sions of a movement which must proceed to
“ C.esar’s Column,” if it does not reach Pisgah.
You are the first among the nations of the
world—are you listening, the world ?—to be
chosen as the champions of a revolt which may
make as readily for the bliss of countless mil-
lions as for their degradation and their destru
tion. Accordingly as you spy out the land will
the countless horde of suffering workers chanv
that step which is now dulled with monotoii
and leaden with the misery of an unrequßp]
burden into the springing quick-march of exul-
tation, or—into the panic-stricken, retrogres
sive rush which would be but a form of suicide
As you spy and as you report of the Promised
Land, as you manifest the capacity, the
worthiness, of the worker to be placed in the van-
guard of the world’s rulers, so will you raise or
depress the spirits oi the great palpitating
human mass. You are not merely represents*
tives of N.S. Wales constituencies — you bei,.
the ark of human hopes !
* * #
For, look you, the eye of every worker of the
peoples which are called (in pitiful derision!
civilised, is fixed upon you. Your success wil
be theirs, your failure will be, alas ! theirs also
Wherever the industrial conflict extends has
travelled the news of your election. I saw a
letter the other day from Norway which
breathed a prayer for your success, and its
sentences, vocal with an eloquent tearfulness
are but the paraphrases of passages to be found
in the Russian, the German, the Italian, the
French, the Swiss, the American newspapers
The workers’ cry that encircles the earth with
one acute moan of anguish has taken a less
piteous note since that eventful June. Where-
ever there is a toiler crushed by the social
machinery—which is the figure we moderns
appropriately use instead of “ the shuttles of the
Fates,” which typified for the ancients their
grinding social forces—there lias been an un-
loosening of the stringency upon his heart, for
your victory is the augury to each of the new
life, of the new condition of things, and has
rightly called forth a burst of heroic emotion
It is something to have done this— something to
have lightened for a moment the universal
distress something to have meshed men’s
hearts over the “whole round globe” within the
subtly-woven filaments of one pure dream.
And that concentration of gaze which you
attracted, that impulse towards a brief happi-
ness which you generated, will be continued
you may be sure, till the area of the industrial
conflict becomes the area of industrial victory,
or—mark the alternative ! —till you are over-
whelmed with failure.
* # *
Should you fall, you will not fall alone. The
desolation of spirit which attaches to failure
will not afflict you or your co-workers in Aus-
tralasia only. Coterminous with the field
which your successes have covered, will be that
of your disaster. You must not fail for this
reason ; you must not add one degree to the
pall of sorrow which o’erhangs the world’s
workers, or one drop of poison to the barb of
their enemies' arrows. Your function is too
momentous, your responsibility too exalted, to
risk defeat. It does not always follow that the
main body of an army will be defeated because
its advanced posts are driven in or annihilated,
but failure in your case and under your condi-
tions will dispirit the industrial army through-
out the world. The Labour-party of the
country where Labour is best off gave us, it
must be assumed, in you their best. Your
defeat will be consequently beyond remedy for
a generation. Defeat of the champions is
always an evil which is relatively irremediable.
A defeat of a mere vedette can be overruled by
the onslaught of the main body, but your
defeat thrusts the main body into confusion.
The destruction of Labour’s elect will send a
throb of exultation through the veins of your
adversaries, giving them new vigour and im-
parting to them that insight and ingenuity
which are both the children and the parents of
successful conquest. But —it is not your
function to inspire your adversaries ; it is your
duty to dispirit, to discourage, and to crush
them. Your forces of inspiration must be
directed to your friends, and not to your foes.
Ihe former you will wrong beyond repair if
you confer upon the latter the supreme gladness
which will flow from your mistakes and your
failures. “Mistakes? Failures? We don’t
intend to make mistakes or to cause failures ;
or to be ranked ourselves as failures ! ” You
may say this ; you, in fact, do say it. You
have been very brilliant successes so far, some
of you think, and, perhaps, you have. Only,
if you have, then we shall have to modify the
ordinary definition of the word.
* P *
If you have succeeded, it is for the first time
in history that a fighting force has conquered
without a general. It is the weak conclusion of
many democrats that democracy implies insub-
ordination and disobedience, and means the
independence always of the individual; that
every democrat is to be another Hal o’ the
Wynd, who always fights for his own hand. I
do not say that most or any of you hold this
very partial opinion, but why should you act
in such a manner as to give rise even amongst
your best friends to the belief that, with you,
democracy means just that and nothing more?
Why have you not elected a captain or a leader?
Why have you not shown the first capacity for
leadership—and you, the chosen of Labour,
are the leaders of Labour—by at once manifest-
ing your qualities of obedience? No man can
rule either himself or his followers unless he
appreciates the educative force that comes from
a just subordination. When you, not bywords,
but by your actions, declare that the idea of
obedience is undemocratic, you simply con-
found two very different things ; Rulership and
Tyranny. It is not undemocratic to obey ;it is
only undemocratic to obey where the governed
have no voice in the election of the governor.
Privilege and caste are founded all the world over
uponextortedobedience, and, therefore, privilege
and caste have to be fought tooth and nail,
upset at the polls, or blown up, if need be,
with dynamite. But obedience and subordina-
tion which are voluntarily rendered are forms
of liberty, and convey the natural recognition
of a law which is interwoven with the very
constitution of the nature of things. You are a
party. The very name of party involves a
voluntary binding together for an end, and
consequently a willing subordination of each
unit to the principle of the whole. In the con-
struction of a party, you thus admit the idea
of subordination. Why, then, refuse, to go one


f
step farther, and exercise that power o
choosing a leader which is the very condition of
fighting? Whoever before heard of a party
ungeneralled ? As well expect a masterless
ship to reach the port as a party to win
without generalship. You are nascent states-
men, and statesmanship is only a variety of
generalship, of that calculation of forces, that
analysis of agents, that discrimination in the
use of tools, that adjustment of means to an
end, that balancing of considerations and con-
jectures, which every leader, be he the captain
of a road-gang or the marshal of an army-corps,
must exert. You, however, would be states-
men without the characteristics of states-
manship, soldiers who object to be led, seamen
who prefer to do without a guiding hand to set
the course and to prick the chart. When, in
the conjunction of human affairs, a crisis
arises it is always the single, the master,, mind
which evolves the decision and strikes out the
spark that ignites the tinder—never the
momentary coalition of a score or two-score of
minds. Now, it is peculiarly your business
to promote crises; you will assuredly fail unless
you create them ; where, then, is your man of
decision? “ He was great, especially in critical
moments,” said Napoleon of Frederick the
Great; “ and that is the finest eulogium one
could pass upon a great character.” Never yet
has a body of men been great in moments of
crisis ; it is always the One.
* * *
Where is your leader? You have not got
one ! Is it because you are torn with petty
jealousies of each other, or is it that you are
one and all convinced that your leader is not
to be found on your roll as it stands to-day ?
Are you waiting for George Reid to withdraw
himself from the aromatic influences of his
afternoon tea-parties at Darling Point, and his
gilded evenings at the Australian Club, to ask
him to place himself at your head ? Or, are
you deferring your choice till the erratic pendu-
lum of B. R. Wise’s ambition once more swings
in your direction? Reid is bidding as ener-
getically as is possible to a man of his
lymphatic temperament for your support ;
Wise is again permitting himself to indulge in
that maudlin academic “ sympathy with the
down-trodden,” which he has not will and back-
bone enough to transform into practical action
on their behalf. Are you waiting for either of
these sawdust-stuffed puppets of circumstance ?
If not, what on earth is the reason for your
refusing to follow the plain procedure suggested
by experience and common-sense? Why not
choose the strongest man among you for a chief,
whoever you may consider him to be, and then
obey him patiently, religiously ?
* # * '
Can it be that you are jealous of one another?
People outside your caucus-room say that it is ;
they say that you are split up into almost as
many cliques as members, that you have little
unity of purpose, and less of mutual sympathy.
1 would rather not believe this rumour, and
would prefer to regard it as an invention of the
enemy. But, what can I believe? What are
the thousands of your constituents who are
looking with anxiety and fear at the eccen-
tricities of your conduct to believe ? We must
believe something, and it is your duty to tell
us, if not in so many words, at least by a dis-
tinctly-defined course of conduct, what really
are the real motives which have led you to
ignore the demands of experience. If J ones is
jealous of Smith, and Smith jealous of Brown,
and Jackson envious of both, how can you hope
to gain that respect from your opponents, and
the outside public, which will be your strongest
weapon to secure the aims of your party and
the enfranchisement of the worker ?
# * *
You have not elected a leader, and your
policy has been incoherent, and your energies
diffused and therefore wasted ; you have not
elected a leader, and each man among you has
placed his own little detached ideas before the
House and the country as the expression of
Labour, when, perhaps, it is nothing of the
sort; you have not elected a leader, and there
has been no proportion in the exercise of your
faculties of speech-making, or essential harmony
between your speech-making and your votes ;
you have not elected a leader, and you have
burdened the records with petty personal ex-
planations and with questions that are out of
occasion and mal apropos. You have not
elected a leader, and you have sunk from
reformers into politicians. No true friend of
yours expected you to strike 12 every time,
but why persistently strike 13 ? That divine
energy of enthusiasm which was yours in the
glorious hour of victory you have frittered
away on a thousand trifles. You allowed
almost at once that magnificent flame to die
away instead of keeping it alight and adding
the fresh fuel to it of self-sacrifice and devotion.
Entering the House to restore its ideals, you
have adjusted yourselves to many of its con-
temptible standards. In this, it is true, you
are only doing what nearly every politician who
preceded you has done, but you were sent into
the Chamber to reverse old methods, abolish
old precedents, and to create the precedent of
remaining pure and patriotic when you had
every temptation to be otherwise. Distinctly,
you have failed up to now, because you have not
seen the necessity of subordination to one single
mind among you. That failure may be the
cause as it is the effect of selfishness. The
selfish politician never was patriotic.
* * *
Selfish individually, you are disintegrated as
a party. Want of cohesion in a militant force
when it is attributable to dissension in the
ranks is treason. Treason is treason always,
and cannot be compared, otherwise I should
say that it were the deepest treason for a force
like yours which enters into the conflict carry-
ing in its knapsacks the destinies of untold
millions, and not merely its own political
fortunes to be internally dissentient. Your
failure, as I have said, will stab to the
heart the worker everywhere; and fill the
worker’s foe with a more hateful, a more
venomous, pride. It will rob the universal
worker of his vitality, and increase the self-
complacency under power of his master. And—
worst of all—it will consolidate the masters as
nothing else could do. Your failure will bind
your opponents more solidly than your success.
The monopolist is a timid creature at the best,
and as he is a bully, you have only to
demonstrate to him that you have the power,
and that you can use it, to induce a cringing so
abject that there will be no name for its
loathsomeness. But show to him that, after
all your chances, you do not know how to use
your strength, and the cowardly bullying of his
past will be the faintest reflection of his
tyranny in the future. Be strong, and you
will make the party you lead strong, and you
may then calculate on the weakness of your
enemy to act as your ally. Divide his forces,
and you conquer him —but how are you going
to send the separating wedge home when your
hand is palsied by ignoble lusts ?
* * *
Try and estimate the strength of your adver-
sary, the capitalist. Look at the Banks —-they
are Ids ; the shipping interests throughout the
world are his; the financial corporations of
every gigantic centre of commerce are his
allies, and every foreign factory with its
colonial depot, which is called ‘‘ an importing
warehouse,” with its parasitical retinue of
retailers, is attached to him also. And, most
malignant of his many heads ! —the Press is Ids.
This is your opponent —hydra-faced, multi-
brained—and these are the contingents of his
armies. Then the Law is his, and Parliament
is still his ; and the Church also. He claims
the world and the flesh and the Devil, and, if
the Churches speak truly, God is his ally also.
So are your necessities, and the necessities of
the men who chose you, and of their wives and
children. The hunger of the artisan’s child,
and the vampire-need of the woman who walks
the street for bread, are his likewise. Now, to
this tremendous panoply of power are you
going to add your weakness, your envy, your
malice, and your uncharitableness? Heavens !
the heart recoils from the mere imagination of
such baseness.
# * *
Hitherto, I will proudly admit, you have not
been base, because I am convinced that, while
you have been weak, your errors have been due
to an ill-acquaintance with the responsibilities
of your position, and the conditions of a poli-
tical contest unequalled in arduousness since
the Reform Bill epoch. You have not hitherto
realised, surely, the magnitude of your task,
the supreme nobility of your opportunity ?
That excuse can now be no longer yours. lam
but a humble scribbler, and my views might be
disregarded, and no blame to you. Neverthe-
less, these are not my views, but the opinions
of many of your sincerest well-wishers and
fellow-workers, and you cannot afford to dis-
regard them, because that discontent with your
achievements, which my words outline, may
burst out into a sudden blaze if you give no
heed to them. I might, indeed, say that I
have a peculiar right to remonstrate with you,
inasmuch as I have suffered in the worker’s cause
to a degree that, I dare venture to say, none of
your number has suffered, though I am not, as the
cant phrase goes, “of your class”—as though
all workers, of hand and of head, were not of
the one class. But I lay no stress upon this
point—l prefer to speak to you as one of many.
Therefore, heed me !
# * *
I know how you are regarded by the men
whom it is your duty and your privilege to
antagonise. I know, too, how they regarded
you three months ago. Between the knowledge
of then and the knowledge of now, how great a
difference ! Then, they feared you ; now, they
despise you, because they think they have
drawn your teeth, or most of them, and those
of the remainder that they cannot draw by
political methods, they intend to extract
socially. They understand now that they have
only to conciliate you as individuals, and not
you as a party. They are going to conquer you
in detail, and their hopes of success a,re, I sub-
mit, not too sanguine. With a patience you
lack, with that subordination for which you
have a contempt, and with that tremendous
organisation of allies of which you own merely
a fraction, they conspire to conquer you—and,
I fear to pen the words—success will probably
wait on their banners while you remain leader-
less. That, I may tell you, is the form of their
calculation. They reckon on your remaining
weak and powerless, while you divest your-
selves of the cohesive intelligence of a general.
Parnell was as much the created as the
creator. You have not your Parnell, but
make him, mould him. The dullest of your
number would be inspired by your thorough
obedience and your enthusiastic co-operation
to mount towards the opportunity. You have
all the one aim; to realise it, you must have
the one soul, the one passion, and the one
method. If you remain weak where you might
be strong, then you will be traitors. In that
event there stands no pedestal of infamy whose
present occupants should not be hauled down
to make way for you. Close up your ranks !
Price Warung.

This list gives the Association away in one

importers, lawyers, brokers, and assorted mil-

National. It represents, on a rough average,
about 5 per cent, of the nation, and it declines
to take in a single representative of the other
95 per cent, because it is the inveterate enemy
of that great balance of the people. It includes
the members of the famous wool-dray proces-
sion, the Soudan-contingent gang, the Jubilee
howlers, the men who packed the so-called

and who would like to see all men who want
their wages raised sent to gaol for “ intimida-
tion.” And that is just how “ National” this
famous Association is.
“ Private Enterprise.”
Sydney D.T., on the project to furnish the City
of Sydney with electric light: —
It is to be observed that undertakings of this kind by a
municipality have a powerful tendency in the direction of
monopoly A monopoly at once checks private
enterprise, and thus deprives the citizens of the benefit
which always accrues from healthy competition.
The fallacy of this argument is seen through its
transparent skin. The here-styled ‘‘mono-
poly ” is in reality a possession owned by the
whole metropolitan population ; the “private
enterprise” and “healthy competition” are
actually “ private monopolies ” of the narrowest
and most hateful kind. It might as well be
urged to semi-Socialistic Australia that the
railways ought to be handed over to “private
enterprise,” inasmuch as they are a “mono-
poly,” which “ deprives the citizens of the
benefits which always accrue from healthy
competition.” Similarly, the same red rag
might be raised regarding water-supply, sewer-
age, main roads, postal carriage, telegraphic
business, etc., etc. The D.T. is uttering the
sheerest of bunkum ; for monopoly, when ex-
tended to a State, or a community, ceases to
be a monopoly and becomes a co-operative
business, in which every citizen is a beneficial
shareholder. The D.T. then proceeds:—
The scheme promoted by the Electric Light and Power
Supply Co., however, does not present the same danger.
It does not ask the Legislature to shut out the competition
of rival enterprises, and the Bill provides further that,
after the expiry of 20 years-a most reasonable period—
the municipality, if it sees fit, may purchase it. This
scheme seems to commend itself as the one most calcu-
lated to serve the public best.
The D.T. evidently holds a brief for this
“ private enterprise,” which doesn’t seek “ to
shut out the competition of rival enterprises,”
and which only wants to have the custom for 20
years of the biggest customer in the city—the
City Council —the customer that will use quite
75 per cent, of all the electric-lighting and
power which the city needs ; it only wants to
have that at a contract price, so that it can
afford, with private firms, to “ cut under” any
opposition ; and, then, it will boldly challenge
all “rival enterprises” to come on and be
squelched ! That, of course, is not a monopoly.
Besides, even after all that, the D.T. has the
gall to point out that the municipality
may purchase the Company’s property after 20
years. But has not the D.T. said that Corpora-
tion ownership will be a “monopoly,” “check
private enterprise,” and stifle “ healthy com-
petition ” ? If it is bad now, how will it be
good in 20 years ? Only in this way : By that
time the Company will have made its pile.
There will then probably be such revolutions in
electrical inventions that its works will be anti-
quated and condemned, and then by log-rolling
and newspaper influence it will sell the useless,
battered, obsolete plant to the city, and let the
people have the monopoly when it is no longer
worth having. The game is too thin.
The Sweater with the Halo.
Judging from present signs, the time is near at
hand when the cheap Salvationist must be put
in the same category as the cheap Chinaman
and the coolie, and be declared a public enemy.
He is fast developing into a “ sweater ” of the
woi’st description, and he is an incurable evil
because he is the slave and minion of the in-
fallible Preacher Booth, and at the orders of
Booth —who sees the necessity of keeping his
red-shirted serf all to himself—he is prepared
to sacrifice every fellow-worker Avho doesn’t
gambol behind the tambourine and wear the
flaring garment of righteousness. A fair
example of this tendency has just come to the
front in Brisbane. In that city the vociferous
race who scream for the Lord has started an
industrial “home,” or some such institution,
where the local “fallen woman” is taken in
and allowed to earn an honest livelihood by
washing. The “ home” is a bright and shining
advertisement for the Army, and its benevolence
in taking in the derelicts of the city leads to
much public enthusiasm. Also, it leads to
many public subscriptions, which is more im-
portant. The “fallen woman,” thus rescued
from the abyss, is worked at high pressure, and
her wages are chiefly food and prayer, and by
keeping the repentant Magdalene at the full
jump, combined with the subscriptions aforesaid,
the Army can do laundry-work at far below the
ordinary rates, and yet make a larger profit
than its competitors. Every mean and pious
so-called “Christian” harridan in the city now
has her work done by the “ fallen woman ” in
one or other of the industrial homes, and gets
her name up —by wearing that sinner to rags—
as a kindly person who patronises a charitable
undertaking. Meanwhile, of the old establish-
ments, which once supplied work and reason-
able wages to several hundred widows and
others, some are closed, and others have had to
lengthen the working hours and severely cut
down the rate of pay. It is all a matter of
competition, and the ordinary laundry, haying
no preaching, and no torch-light procession,
and no collection-plate attached to it, is worse
off, even when wages have been cut down one-
half, than its pious rival. But the cast-iron
Salvationist snuffles in a vicious circle, and sees
nothing beyond it. Work is scarce in Brisbane
at present, and life is terribly hard, and every
time the Prophet of the Poster and his followers
take in some lost female, and make her work
hard, and turn her into a huge advertise-
ment, and use her for a machine to cut down
wages, they thereby wreck some female who
wasn’t lost before, or deprive some other
woman of her billet and leave her un-
provided for and hungry. It is no use
remonstrating with the Army on such a
point as this. No country is large enough
to hold an individual who is answerable to
nobody save William Booth. The question at
issue is the old convict-labour question in a new
and aggravated form, for the saved female of
the hallelujah band is even cheaper than the
gaol worker, and she undermines an industry
which is already about the hardest and the
most unprofitable in existence, and as an addi-
tional outrage she is passed off on the gullible
public as a moral and praiseworthy enterprise
and an act of charity. If sweating is unavoid-
able, let us at least have the sweater without a
halo; the sweater with the halo is unendurable.
Bertram Everard, the young and well-to-do
man convicted of aggravated criminal assaults
on several females at Sydney, has been sen-
tenced to imprisonment for life—the first three
years in irons. Everard’s history consists of
one long and shocking series of such offences.
Several of his near blood-relatives are in lunatic
asylums, and that he himself is a crank is
evidenced pretty clearly by his previous history,
and by the fact that, though he had been in
gaol before for such offences, he committed
most of his outrageous later deeds in public
(and on women who were in the company of
other women), without making any preparations
whatever for getting away. It is, in one aspect,
the old case of “ Monster Williams ” over
again. It is also, in another aspect, the Colston
case over again. The man probably knew that
he was doing wrong, but he “ had to do it,”
and the law still pretends to believe that there
is no such thing known to medical science (and
to ordinary common-sense) as irresistible im-
pulse, the outcome of a diseased will. Everard's
case is one for a hospital or asylum, not for a gaol.
The pompous homily of Judge Fitzhardinge,
in sentencing to life-imprisonment and irons this
wretched satyr, was worthy only of the days
of witchcraft. It may be urged that it really
matters very little whether Everard be sent to
prison or to an asylum. It matters a whole
lot. When you send a man to prison, you
intend to punish him ; when you send him to
an asylum, you wish to protect him and other
people. That such a creature should be solemnly
put in irons is an outrageous practical joke.

In Melbourne the other day a dishonest young
man was sent to gaol, and when he was in
prison his sweetheart endeavoured—contrary
to regulations —to pass him a note. The con-
tents of the incendiary document were chiefly
as follows ;
I will start to work as soon as I can, and I will save as
much money as I can until you come home agrain, so as
you will have a home to call your own ; and then, love,
if I do that vou will promise to leave off knocking about
with the fellows you have been with.
The poor wretch of a girl didn’t succeed, as it
happened, in smuggling the note to the bowled-
out creature on whom she had wasted her truly
womanly affections. The usual capacious
policeman stopped her, and lodged a
charge of vagrancy against her for the
attempt. Of course it would have been just as
much to the point if he had charged her with
bigamy and aggravated horse-stealing, or with
being the Kelly gang, or with piracy on the
high seas combined with unlawfully jumping an
alluvial claim—any one of them would have
been as much like the real circumstances of the
case as the other. But vagrancy is a handy
and elastic charge (especially as applied to a
woman who is doing her poor little miserable
best to reform a man) and it enables any police-
man to tramp in a general way on anybody
he doesn’t like. And on the strength of tins
charge a bench of remorseless jackasses sent the
girl to gaol for six months !
Sydney, people will tell you, is a I reetrade
port, and Melbourne is under the dominion
of a grinding system of Protection. In Sydney
the R.M.S. Arcadia has to pay £164 15s.
pilotage and harbour and light dues in and out
each trip; the M.M. Co.’s Polynesien,
£154 16s. ; the Orient Qo.’s Orizaba, £l7l 35.;
the North German Lloyd’s Kaiser Wilhelm,
£226 12s. In the year 1858, when small
vessels only traded to Australia, these fees
were fixed at 4d. per ton, and at 4d. per ton
they remain, unto this day. Yet in Melbourne
none of these vessels has to pay more than £5O,
and the service rendered extends over 50 miles,
instead of, as at Sydney, over, say eight miles.
In other words, Freetrade Sydney charges
three times as much for one-sixth of the service
—i.e., the Sydney charges are 1800 per cent,
over those of the protected port of Melbourne !
As pointed out by Mr. Keigwin, the charges
from Yarmouth to London —a distance of 120
miles, which sometimes takes a week or so—are
only £24 for a fully-loaded vessel drawing 24
feet. Why is so much charged at Sydney and
Newcastle? Simply because the “ Freetrade”
Government get revenue out of it. Ihe present
annual cost of the service is £18,333, and the
receipts £38,689, leaving a profit of £20,356,
obtained by a gross and insensate species of
Protection. What does the Pro-trade-I ree-
lection Ministry think of the situation as thus
stated ?

M v Dkar, Moorarixua,—
Melbourne's indictment that Sydney hasn't any un-
sectarian gangs of women going round and chivvying the
lowah ordahs, almost makes my pen smile. You can’t
open your umbrella in Sydney—especially on a Monday—
without spitting a member of this, that, and the other
society on it, who greets you with a heavenly expression,
and her hands full of flowers, or tracts, or food, or old
women’s petticoats. The place is literally alive with
them. They scurry down the Block—that popular
thoroughfare seems to he the way to everywhere, some-
how—about 2 p.m. attractively gnt-np and not humping
more “comforts” than are compatible with a stylish
outline against the horizon, and delve off in all directions
to tiie dwellings of the “pooah.” I went with one of
them one day and was greatly edified with the result.
There was a lot of pious snuffling and “Gord” (though,
by the way, I shouldn't put it in turned commas since
Lady J. pronounces it so) was praised in a loud voice
■ when a pound of tea or a flannel garment was handed
out ; hut, as a rule, the good little hooks and the nose-
gays were tolerated in a resigned, hurt sort of way which
w ould have withered all my philanthropy on the spot- had
I been the donor. No “ organised ” feminine poor-rangers
in Sydney! All one’s fingers and toes aren’t numerous
enough to add up only the tabby societies on.
More of it! It is cabled that “ The marriage was cele-
brated to-day (14th inst.) of Miss Ida Lee, daughter of
Mr. George Lee, of Bathurst, N.S.W., with Mr. Charles
Marriott.” All the well-known plans and specifications of
the happy woman, but just the bald visiting-card label of
the happy man ! As a matter of useful fact the excite-
ment ought to he all the other way. Antipodean society
generally knows all about the Lidy from her first Loth
np’ards, and all the curiosity is as to “Who” she has
married. In the present case there are fears that she lias
thrown herself away on the Charles Marriott who was
wont to compose discordant dance-music (for ladies’
fashion-papers) at half-a-guinea a sheet.
One looks for sucli remarks from the lady contributors
to alleged democratic daily papers, who all generally
exhibit symptoms of dying to he English, hut you hardly
expect the sports in the sporting column of Sydney D.T.
to dub so-and-so the “sporting squire.” There are no
squires in Australia, as lots of people deeply deplore ; also,
no rustics to hang on their forelocks and run like hares to
open the gate to let “ t' squoire ” through. What joy it
would mean for some people—especially for some women
if a race arose of little territorial tin-gods with a genuine
chawbacon following, instead of these horrid “hands”
who have to be politely entreated to let down a slip-rail!
It is deeply interesting to learn, amidst the eagerness
shown by local O.M.G.’s and such-like decorated cattle to
hang their little tin-pot stars and ribbons and tinsel all
over their puffy persons whenever the ghost of an oppor-
tunity offers—that top-grit at ’Ome rather shies at
“honours” which only mean a big milliner's bill to the
unhappy recipient, and tries only to merit some cheap
and undressy titular favour which it can carry about in
an ordinary tail-coat. Indeed, such a horror of the two-
penny-coloured has seized upon the Hot Tong, that, on
dit. more than one earl hasn't git a real coronet in the'
whole stretch of his wardrobe, and would have to balance
a “slop” circlet if life were ordered suddenly to turn-out
in full ceremonious fig.
Talking about “ honours,” 1 hear that it is not only the
lovely effect of the star against pa’s white-shirt buzzum
that makes such distinctions hankered after by the
leaders of local sassiety— it is said that there is a per-
centage on the house-bills in it to a smart woman who
knows how to cheese-pare with shopkeepers even though
their barouche waits outside. One Australian gang,
recently abased to a knighthood and without much spot
cash to carry on the “ honours,” sent notices to all their
tradespeople setting forth that the benefit of Lady Blank’s
patronage ought to mean a reduction of so much in the
pound, especially as Lady Blank would always recom-
mend, i:c. I don’t know if the tradespeople “ bit," hut
ever since this anecdote has been in circulation 1 have
gazed obliquely at the fashionable lady who is so anxious
to tell you that Grubhins fitted out her drawing-room—
so moderately, too ; or that Miss Filoselle fitted the dress
you admire so much : you really ought to try Filoselle.
If you mention my name, &c. It is then that the worldly-
wise wink their other eye.
Sometimes May and December matches turn out a
winning speculation for the lady—which is when the
gilded groom goes under in short order. An instance
happened in Sydney very recently when a used-up,
dissipated, old money-hugger married a sweet confiding
innocent of 20, and then suddenly demised after four
weeks of connubial elysium, leaving his disconsolate
relict £30,000 hard cash, and some houses and things.
That is the very happiest denouement for a Mammon
marriage, because, you see, when old men marry young
wives they often adore them for six months or so while
wifey is fresh to the collar and on her best behaviour, but
sometimes after that they commence to fix their faded
eye suspiciously on a possible Petrarch, and the result is
a harrowing codicil leaving Mary Jane 30s. a week as the
reward of her spirited matrimonial spec., while the big
sum goes to raise a home for lost dogs, or a Sunday-
school.
Sydney Ladies’ Sanitary Association was deeply
interested the other week in Dr. F. A. Bennett's lecture
on the skin and hair, and if he hadn’t generalised so
much he could have raised up a Bennett cult there and
then. One thing he said was that he would show the
causes of-blushing, at which the whole crowd sat up—to
learn how to manage their vaso-motor nerves. But you
could have heard a hair drop when the learned man went
on to paints and powders (by *’ way I had a notion that
.1 could tell who was—er—renovated and who wasn’t when
the doctor was speaking about makes-up—the first-
named dropped their eyes, and sometimes their ears
turned red- the behaviour of the others was normal),
saying that, although he didn't advocate the use of paints
and powder, “some kinds didn't materially injure the
face or complexion ’’-—which was vastly encouraging, only
that he didn’t specify t’other from which, and therefore
patched loveliness was as far off the hygienic track as
ever.
And this is just where I come in. The other day (in the
public interest alone, mind you) I interviewed the medi-
cine man and implored him to tell me what was really the
safest way to put on the poaches and cream? and he
said: “ All powders, if used excessively, pit the skin, hut
otherwise no harm results from powders made of finely-
powdered rice, starch, wheat, flour, Indian corn, orris-
root, from the vegetable world ; while harmless minerals
are line oxide of zinc, horacic acid, bismuth and silicate
of magnesia. As for paints, one ought to he careful what
■ one uses for fear of blood-poisoning, hut two of the most
harmless pigments are the sediment resulting firm the
action of spirits of lavender on talc, making a good white
paint, and pure carmine for red colouring.” Now, oh my
sisters, that you have counsel's opinion on the breathless
subject, don't steep your dear little ncses in French pink
powders, nor your cheeks in Chinese rouge any more,
please!
But, talking about face-lectures, they're rather waste
enthusiasm, after all. No amount of scientific combing
and brushing and sponging will ever make the average
Aiistralieiine the bright, firm-cheeked, cherry-ripe sort of
darling one calls the “ English type,” and meets locally,
now and again only, in some parts of New England and
Tasmania. Our sapless cheeks, alas ! are the fault of our
dragging climate—not because we don’t tub regularly
and curry-comb our faces religiously upwards from chin
to cheek-bone in an unflagging endeavour to rub the
wrinkles out.

didn't, although the “fact” was duly cabled and every-
body expected to see two bonnets come over the border.
By the way, Lady J. seems to be a self-reliant personage
who doesn’t want an imported female to help her bear up
under an Australian accent, or to sit side by side in the
theatres and to mystify outsiders as to which of’em is
’er. Australia will he glad to see Lady Galloway—so long
as she doesn’t bring along Lord Galloway, whose reputa-
tion has preceded him.
Melbourne seems to possess more attractions for the
roving female relatives of its Governor than Sydney docs
in a similar way. Just compare. Gov. Hopetoun has
had his wife’s sister, his own two sisters, and now his
cousin, Lady Willoughby, to cheer his exile. Carington
brought his wife’s sister out when he came, but no other
sister, cousin or aunt followed in her footsteps or was
lured by her tale, and no lady relative of Lord Jersey has
so far showed signs of bounding over the deep bine sea
Australia-wards. Can this vice-regal female predeliction
for Colllns-street mean that the fatal fascinations of the
Victorian Johnnie are getting an English reputation ?
The State schools held their annual concerts at Sydney
Town Hall on 12th and 13th inst., but I denied myself the
pleasure of attending, perhaps because the magnetic
Lady J. didn’t go, and the attraction was lacking. Any-
way, a mob of over-dressed infants yelling Lady Jersey’s
poetry set to music docs not soothe the savage scrihess,
and the spectacle of excited parents in the audience
exchanging handkerchief waves with their darlings on
the platform so depressed me last year that I didn't
repeat the experiment this. But I had the felicity of
coming home in the tram with six of the performers the
other night. Four of the six were visibly rouged, and one
was lightly lead-pencilled round the eyelids, yet none
were over fifteen years of age. All behaved badly,
giggling and crowding and mouthing and signalling to
male companions in the smoking-car. Two whispered
together with girlish titterings, and the display of faded
roses, at the end of which the listener loudly remarked :
“ But, Cis, he’s ma-a-rried but “ Cis,” who looked four-
teen, only carried her confidences still further. Now, I
do not blame these girls for their conduct, since they
were let loose after the excitement of a concert to find
their own way home, hut one does feel surprised at
“parents and guardians,” as the circulars say, not think-
ing it worth their while to sheep-dog their charges on
such occasions. However, a mother who could let her
young daughter paint up would hardly object if the whole
world was shocked at her antics afterwards.
V.R. night at the Royal last Saturday was rather wet'
blanketed by the absence of the two leading ladies of the
occasion—Farren through indisposition in the ankle, and
Lady JerseyUrom a se -ions attack of staying away.
Nevertheless, H.E. was there, ciceroning a male party of
three, squired by Chumley. Ethel Blenheim took Barren's
part. What would happen if Ethel got the influenza
is difficult to foresee, as she seems to he understudy to
the whole crowd, and took Sylvia Grey’s role the other
evening when that divinity (who has an unpleasant trick
of turning her hack on the audience, by the way) was
chained to her downy couch. The whirligig went well, as
usual, Ethel only having to be told to knock Leslie’s hat
off in the last act ; also, the tarantula-legged Storey has
to kick higher to get his slim, pink limbs over this
Cinder's head in the dance-duet, since Ethel is taller than
Nellie. The jokes seem to he wearing well, and the point
is never lost on a tram-haunting public when Danhy
explains, rs lie tries to light his pipe in the girl-filled
schoolroom, “I thortit was a smokin’ compartment!”
Myra- Kemble has had .a good time up North with “ Dr.
Bill,” frizzled by the climate, hut, as a set-off, pelted with
bouquets from ecstatic crowds, some of whom brought
and labelled their own chairs, ready. Not only bouquets
were thrown, hut, at Charters Towers, an admirer threw
a bundle of 100 paid-up shares in a new mine to Myra, who
is seen off by admiring crowds every time she strikes
camp. Only one untoward incident has so far occurred
on the trip. On board a tender in Keppel Bay the deck
awning blew down and brought a big oil-lamp with it, and
if Myra hadn’t given an agile skip and fallen on her face,
her funeral would very likely have occurred last week.
As it was, she was rescued smiling like a seraph from
among the wreck of tarpaulin. A Brisbane season with a
week of “Jane,” and then overland to Newcastle is the
future plan of chain—l mean campaign.

The funniest trades-union I ever heard of is theassocia-
tion of “upper female servants” of the wealthy people
of Polls and Darling Points, Sydney. The membership
consists of 40 or thereabouts, and is exclusively composed
of the domestics of “ kerridge families.” The minimum
wage is £1 a week, and they always get it. One of
the conditions of membership is an obligation not to
accept service except with a family that keeps a
footman and a carriage and pair, and consequently the
household which can only boast a livery-stable brougham
and screw is utterly beyond the pale. The most material
part of the organisation is that when a member is out of a
“ place” through the family going “ home” or removal to
the country, or when she wants a holiday, the other
members pay for her hoard and residence for a specified
time at a house in Darlinghurst, kept by an old-time
retaiew of one of the first families.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The Mayor of Ballarat refuses to allow the
Salvation Army to parade its streets. He says
be “cannot find any general named Booth on the
official British Army list.” That mayor would

For pre-eminent excellence of composi-
tion, Marshall's Tonic Nervine was awarded the
gold medal against all competition at the. great
Centenary Exhibition. Whether it deserved this
honour one cannot say, but it certainly is in great
demand by convalescents for its unrivalled tonic
properties.

The Bulletin notes with considerable
regret that Edmund Barton is not going to be the
new Premier of N.S.W. It doesn’t suppose for a
moment that Barton would be so good a Premier
as Dibbs, but it wanted to try how his bland
countenance would go in a picture. Barton’s
frontispiece is large and hairless, and The Bulle-
tin artist, who has been drawing Parkes so long,
said that he really wanted to have a turn at a
clean-shaven man for the sake of a little rest and
variety.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabetli-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEV SHOWS FOII COMING WEEK.
Criterion' “Society.”
Theatre Royal “Gaiety Co.”
Her Majesty's “ The Dancing Girl.”
Garrick "The Librarian.”
Theatrical business in Sydney is at a very
low ebb just now, and “The Dancing Girl,” so
far, has not occasioned a flood-tide at Her
Majesty’s pay-boxes. This play might, on the
whole, be more fitly called “The Preaching Girl,”
for Sybil makes much more use of her tongue than
does Drusilla of her feet. It is a well-written
drama, but asks its audiences to take too much
on trust. “The wicked duke” is disappointing,
because he gives no outward evidence of turpitude.
Further, the story is occasionally infested with
soliloquy, chiefly on the part of the noble idiot,
and invariably weighted down with dialogue!
Likewise, the lame girl seems to have been intro-
duced only to haunt thefootsteps of the peer, while
whispering in his aristocratic auricle broken-backed
allegories concerning “horses’hoofs” and “broken
bowls.” The bowl—an elevenpence-half-penny
one—is broken in sight of the audience, and it is
well that Mr. Alderson is a good shot with a sofa
cushion, for the play could positively not proceed
without the allegory, and the allegory couldn’t be
dragged in without the smashed china. The
strong point about the present production is the
staging. Brunton’s scenery in all three sets is
worth locking at, and some of the costumes are
absolutely gorgeous. As for the acting, the
honours arc chiefly appropriated by three per-
formers. Julius Knight’s John Christian is
vigorous and natural ; George P. Carey as David
Ives, the father of the dancing girl, is equally
good, with smaller opportunities, and the Sybil
Crake of Gwendoline Floyd furnishes additional
evidence that this London-journally titled young
lady is likely to do some good for herself. In the
lead, Laura Villiers is, as usual, dainty and
nice, but sorely lacking in power and handicapped
with her inflexible voice and stereotyped arm-
flapping gestures. Clifton Alderson also runs in
nits with bis gesticulation and arouses antagonism
with the male portion of his audiences by looking
unutterably lovely and talking in a finicking, don-
cherkuow. Cockney fashion. Otherwise, the stage
is chiefly given up to inarticulate, unhappy, and
jerky geniuses who wobble on and off simply
because the prompter told them so.
«- * *- *
“ Ours,” the story of an unreasonably
jealous woman, her shortlegged husband, a
wealthy booby, who says mighty smart things, an
ingenious and an ingenuous maiden, a rare done
leg of mutton, and an alleged roley-poley pudding,
with some slight verbal improvements on the
author’s text, tills the bill just about as
fully as on former occasions at Sydney
Criterion. Geo. Titheradge, of course, plays the
brewer—who is always sleeping when he is not
soliloquising—admirably, though all bis old man-
nerisms and Ids old shooting-suit are very much in
evidence. Marie Fraser, as Blanche Hoye,
looked nicer than pie, but lias apparently no sing-
ing voice, and so the little points to be made by
the love-ballad with the tears in the voice, the
falter, and the breakdown, were not scored. Percy
Lyndal repeats a former success, as docs Pattie
Browne, but Nellie Murtyne, who replaces Miss
Romer, looks too youthful and is not sufficiently
dignified as Lady Shendryn. Arthur Lawrence
is perfectly lovely as the old General, but has the
misfortune to succeed Cathcart in apart for which
the latter was physically constructed. Dot Bouci-
cault does not fully fill his partner’s Russia leather
slippers as Prince Perovsky ; he is precise, diminu-
tive, without presence, and suggests an articled-
clerk rather than an absolute autocrat. As Ser-
geant Jones, in George Anson’s absence, MTntyre
gets on better than could have been expected.
Minor parts are not particularly ably filled, but
the scenery of Messrs. Spong and Churchward is
a poem in paint. “Society,” by no means
Robertson’s best, on Saturday next.
*# * *
“Transported for Life ” is well-known to
Sydney audiences as a blood-curdling, marrow-
freezing histrionic hash-np of murder, love,
villainy and heroism, ending as usual in the tardy
triumph of down-trodden virtue over blatantly-
assertive vice. The scene fluctuates between a
life of luxury an I that of a diggings, and the
variety show of the latter spot, with its songs by
Mrs. Darrell and Messrs. Shine and Watson, was
hailed by “the gods” on opening night as a
pleasing innovation. Of course, George Darrell
is the manly and unjustly accused Hubert
Chandos, and equally, of course, Mrs. Darrell is
the lovely and faithful Edith Vaughan, and both
get away with most of the buns in the Garrick
bakery. Arthur Greenaway is all right as the
villain, and other parts are handled by Constance
Deorwyn, Marie Brooks and Miss Clitberoe.
“ East Lynne ” will be played on Friday evening
for Christine Darrell’s benefit.

George Leitch, having rested sufficiently,
will rise again next Saturday at Sydney Garrick
and produce “ The Librarian ” —the original of
“The Private Secretary,” and claimed to be more
humorous even than that sad curate with the
goloshes. Also, he is said to be more dank, more
boneless, and more unobtrusive than the other
version, and is driven about harder by Fate and
swept to and fro more helplessly on the tide of
ettcumstanoes. Like the secretary he has a pro-
found longing for buns—“Oh, that it would rain
buns,” he wails in his hunger and grief—and when
his lunch is rent from him he grows terrible in his
feroeitj 7 . These facts can be verified next Satur-
day by anyone who tenders the customary
pecuniary consideration at the door, for the
encouragement of a brilliant and deserving
comedian. Even the sight of George Leitch’s
bright smile is worth the money, and the indi-
vidual who wouldn’t pay to see it is a clod with no
poetry in his soul.
* * * Si-
lt is a melancholy fact that the majority
of Melbourne playgoers like best that kind of
tragedy in which a howling lunatic in check pants
plays the lead, his support consisting of two
supers in tin armour representing the Flower of
the Spanish army, and five weird and ancient
chorus-girls consisting chiefly of bones, The
Alexandra, with its present show, deserves
better houses, whenever a work like “ Rigoletto,”
“Lucia,” “Ernani” or “Traviata” is staged,
and, worse luck, it doesn’t get them. Signora
Cuttiea, who makes The Bulletin wish it were
dead when she essays comedy, is as great
a Yioletto Valery as she was a Lucia, or
an Elvira. Her acting in the death-scene was
a finished thing, and when, with the last bubble
of life, she shook off her mourners and striding
down the stage gave the last few bars of the
magnificent music, and, shrieking, fell into Ger-
mont’s arms and died, a great yell arose from the
house, and the curtain came up again in response.
Signorina Tresani and Miss Freeman were satis-
factory as Flora and Amina, and Germont’s
music was well sung by Signor Cuttiea. But be
it understood there isn’t anybody in the show
after the lady who makes up so badly and
sings so well. The mountings are, as
usual, not good enough for a circus; and
when Traviata’s home is shown as a lightning-
blasted humpy witli cheap lace curtains on the
windows, and a half-crown quilt on the bed, and the
bed itself tied up with string, one decides that it
pays just as well to be virtuous and have nice
carpets to walk on, and to sleep on a strictly moral
couch with pink satin hangings. Sara Bern-
hardt playing in a caravan would be con-
sidered an insult. But Cuttiea warbling in
a coffee-stall is evidently thought a perfectly
consistent thing. Monday night he, Cuttiea,
benefited, and “Traviata” and “Ernani” were
repeated during the week. “Faust” and
“ Carmen ” are to go up before the close of the
season, which now draweth very nigh. Mean-
while, “Lucrezia” goeth on the programmes of
this (Thursday) and to-morrow nights,

At the Royal, business is not keeping up
to last week’s standard, and the water in the
tank is not so damp as it was on first night.
Likewise Geo. Rignold has achieved additional
embonpoint and prosiness since last notice, and
the “Lights o’ London ” burn as if they were fed
with condemned kerosene. For all that the
masses recognise the “Lights” as the best
existent drama of the class, and, at any rate, good
paying houses are common. It is about time
though that the mouldy theory that a melo-
dramatic hero can knock 87 policemen and a
naval brigade crooked and endways was exploded.
Mrs. Geo. Rignold plays Mrs. Jarvis pleasantly,
and evidently is seen at her best in old woman
parts.
** * %
At the Princess, Maggie Moore still talks
with a cold “id her dose,” and “Our Flat”
collects a few of the scarce shekels in the hands of
the publ’c. Edwin Kelly, once sacred to dashing
quasi-comic military parts, has now settled down
to playing eccentric fathers in frock-coats and a
white curly wig, and our heart aches for those
symmetrically-padded legs through which all the
girls of long ago fell in love with him. Those
girls are now married, and some of them are
divorced, and Edwin plays old eccentric ancestors
with a bad grace.

J.C. W. puts in a short season at the big
house with “Kerry,” and “Rip Van Winkle,”
opening next Saturday. This may be intended
either as a stop-gap or a treasury recouper, but,
under any circumstances, it is bound to be the
latter. J.C.W. is a good all-round actor and a
careful caterer for Australia generally, and is too
much liked to be able to avoid great houses. The
only way he could scare off an audience is by
shooting at it.
* Sf * »
After the trashy comedies of the Princess,
“New Men and Old Acres,” at the Bijou, is cheer-
ing to the soul. The plot of “ New Men ” is essen-
tially simple. Some busted-up aristocrats named
Vavasour are being fired out of the Abbey, their
ancestral hall. There is not enough in the family
strong box to buy a drink with, and so it is
designed to sell pretty Lilian Vavasour to any
person who can table sufficient greed to raise the
mortgage on the crumbling wall and the unhealthy
moat, and free from encumbrance the collection of
tortured bones in the donjon keep. The party
who is expected to provide the coin for all this is
Sam Brown, a wealthy Liverpool merchant,
alleged to have made a big rise in hides or some-
thing smelling equally as bad. For all that, he is a
far better fellow than old Vavasour, who puts on airs
to himself, and privately wails over the comedown
his family has endured. Lilian is slightly in love
with Sam Brown, apart from the monetary con-
siderations, and they become engaged. But a few
days before the intended wedding Brown’s firm
gets into difficulties, or Brown’s partner skips, or
something like that, and a commercial friend in
the shape of a land boomer and company-promoter
fellow named Bunter offers to advance £40,000 on
the Abbey mortgages which he (Brown) holds.
Bunter has discovered the Abbey lands to be full
of rich iron lodes, but when he tries to realise on
his mortgage he finds old Vavasour knows all
about the iron lodes, Brown having acquainted
him therewith. So Brown pulls thro’ and the iron
is sold retail for tuppence a pound or something
like that, and Lilian marries her nearly almost
deadbroke lover, and Bunter dies of the disap-
pointment. Mrs. Brough makes a very fine study
of Lilian Vavasour and all the business, especially
in the scenes with the phlegmatic Sass as Sam
Brown, was as good as anything she has done
before. Bunter, in the hands of G. W. Anson, is
an unctuous person who would recite psalms and
go through a drunken man at the same time,
Corlesse as the financier is very satisfactory. Miss
Romer and Miss Taylor are very good as Lady
Bunter and Mrs. Bunter respectively, and Eille
Norwood as Bertie Fitz Urge is tiresome and a
blot on the bill.

Siiazolle lias bill-sticked Melbourne to the
effect that Cup week shall see him burst out in all
his glory in his illustrated version of “ Enoch
Arden.” These one-man shows, when they are
really good, hardly ever fail to bring grief to
the ambitious entertainment with its tons of prin-
cipals and scenery, and its hundreds of auxiliaries.
And as Snazelle is unapproachable as a one-man
showman, we may expect something very fine.
* & *
At the Adelaide Royal “ The Old Guard,”
or rather Billy Elton, has been tickling the
risible faculties of the public to a consider-
able extent, bub “la grippe” has marked several
of the vocalists for its prey, and made them bark
woefully. Also the same disease has decimated
the audience, which shows awful signs of diminu-
tion. Therefore the management revive “The
Gondoliers” “by special request” for a few
nights, for even the dying influenza victim will
crawl out of his bed to see that bright opera once
more, and the takings will lookup. “The Yeo-
man of the Guard ” follows. Taking the season
through, the business has been really good, and
Pete Hughes strokes his bald pate with satisfac-
tion. Fitzgerald’s Circus has opened, and at
Garner’s Rooms, Professor Anderson, “The
Wizard of the North,” (how many of them are
there?) is performing.

We insist on seeing J. C. Williamson and Maggie Moore
again as John and Lizzie Stofel. Neither is so young as

Box of cigars thrown on the stage, last week, in Mel-

Christie Murray told a Bulletin London correspondent
that he hopes in a few weeks to have a theatre, and to
start “Ned’s Chum” for a run. He has one or two other
plays in his bag which he commenced in Australia, and
which he will finish forthwith. They are, he says, built
on a broader basis than “ Ned’s Chum,” and will contain
more seriously-purposed work. Christie is in great
request just now, and he has more work than he can well
get through—magazine-fiction for the most part. What
between stage management, acting, writing, and other
w'ork, he says he feels himself a played-out man for the
moment.
Haddon Chambers thinks the play he is now working at
for Thorne is the best he has ever written, and will make
his name famous among English dramatists. So may it be.
We never protest against the limelight till the moon
follows an actor round the stage.
Gounod’s statement that Melba will soon surpass all
appears to be on the road to fulfilment. It is cabled that
she is parleying for £BOO a night during an American
trip, and has appointed Lord Rothschild and Earl de
Grey as trustees of the money she is settling on her little
son.
Minnie Palmer at latest was about to produce, at the
Vaudeville, London, Mark Ambient’s “ L’Oncle CMestin.”
The Referee, organ of dramatist Sims, says that
Christie Murray, in his play, “Ned’s Chum,” violates
the fundamental dramatic principle of never keeping a
secret from your audience. The play, it remarks, was
not a brilliant failure, for it was not a failure nor was it
brilliant, but it was so mitigated a success that it is un-
likely to be seen again in London. The Sporting Times,
on the other hand, says that the play was an unqualified
success, and will doubtless soon take its place regularly
on the bill at some West End theatre.

Outside a stationer’s shop in Elizabeth-street, Melb., is
a show-case, and on it is the inscription, “ Hymns,
ancient and modern.” The contents of the case are
photos., several hundreds of them. Most of them are
ladies in tights, and the rest are ladies who have appar-
ently lost their tights. Somebody pointed out the dis-
crepancy the other day, but still above this gang there
glares out, like an all-seeing eye or a. banner with a
strange device, the words “ Hymns, ancient and modern."

On Thursday last, the Minerva Dramatic Society played
“ Love Wins,” a three-act comedy by Savile Clark and de
Tensau, peculiarly adapted to the uses of amateurs, in
the private theatre at “Oakwood,” Glebe Point, Sydney,
the residence of Mrs. Dwyer, before a fashionable audi-
ence, in number over a hundred, the bulk of whom
remained till daylight to take part in the later festivities
of supper and ball. The acting laurels on this occasion
were garnered by Mr. E. J. Redman, the honorary
stage-manager and scene-painter, who, in the lead, ac-
quitted himself creditably, while the wreaths that remained
were annexed by Miss Tompson, as “ old woman,” Miss
Meotti(who comes of an acting family), as the good fairy,
and Mr. J. O. M. Skelton, president of the club, as the
low comedy “old man.”

London, and so does the Paris Archbishop in his city.
Why ? Archbishop Goold allowed her to sing at St.

J. C. Williamson, born 1845, at Mercer, Pennsylvania,
where he early studied the Pennsylvania Dutchman.
Began at Milwaukee Theatre, Wisconsin. Afterwards
Wallack’s, N.Y., then the Broadway, under Barney Wil-
liams. Back to Wallack’s. Next, California Theatre,
’Frisco, under M’Cullough. Had “Struck Oil” recast
from a MS. called “The Deed, or Five Years Away.”
Played it for farewell benefit. Everyone advised him not
to open in “Struck Oil” in Melbourne. After doing
Australia, the Williamsons played under G. B. W.-Lewis’
management, Calcutta. Opened at London Adelphi,
Easter Monday, 1876.

are not in it with the two great stars.

Wilson Barrett, before adopting the sock and buskin,
was a mercantile clerk in London. Arthur Roberts was a
lawyer’s clerk there, and Frank Thornton used to beguile
the tedium of his occupation as a soft-goods clerk by
occasional appearances in small parts, until he finally
blossomed into a professional actor. Harry Hunter, one
of the Mohawk minstrels, was a tea-taster in Mincing-
lane ; Jerome K. Jerome, a goods-clerk at the Broad-
street station ; Henry Pettit, ditto on the Midland Rail-
way office in Whitecross-street; whilst G. R. Sims was a
clerk in his father’s counting-house in Aldersgate-street.
Add to these Sydney Grundy, Edgar Bruce, and Herman
Merivale—all three barristers with chambers in the
Temple - and it will be seen that the City is a pretty good
dramatic recruiting-ground.

steeds from the corporation carts and ’buses, deli-
berately passed over the hatchet-faced sisters with
the flat chests and chicken legs, who had toiled
and thumped for the Army since the day it started
here, and gave the coveted mounts to a lot of raw
she-recruits, whose sole recommendations for the
doubtful honour lay in their pink cheeks, bright
eyes, and plump, well-rounded forms. Had the
affair been a burlesque leg-show company’s caval-
cade this would have been the correct thine
to do.” B

Had the branches of the legal profession
been amalgamated, W. J. Trickett would have
