Arthur Francis Wood, who, as Govern-
ment surveyor, originally laid out Rockhampton
(Q.), in ’5B, and who had ever since identified
himself with the varying fortunes of the city, died
on 14th October, aged 64 years. He was at one
time a wealthy local property-owner, but he was
the sort of man who pinned wings on to his riches.
General Booth proposes to secure a certain
huge abandoned London market (erected by
Baroness Coutts) for the sale of Australian frozen
meat. The latter is now landed in London at two
pence per pound, and the London butchers retail
it at nearly, if not quite English prices, and often
as English beef. Booth proposes to undersell
these butchers and if possible get a monopoly of
the meat-trade !
For the first time for nearly 30 years.
Mount William, from the summit of which
Mitchell made some of his most important ob-
servations, was covered with snow last week. It
was by tracing O’Mealley’s tracks after a fall of
snow on Mount William, that that notorious
bushranger was captured, after he had defied the
police—and police were fine bushmen in those
days—for several months,
B. D. Morehead apparently touts for the
R.O. vote by saying that “ Romanists have been
shamefully treated under the Queensland Educa-
tion Act.” He adds a little side-spout for the
Protestant church-vote by urging that the Bible
be read in schools. “This secular system of
education,” says he, “ will yet bring a great
disaster upon the colony.” When twitted with an
old-time fiery speech of his against State-school
Bible reading, Morehead calmly explained that
he had then referred to the Old Testament only,
adding that an expurgated edition of the Bible is
sadly needed. “Let the Old Testament go, let
it be burnt, but the New Testament, or portions
of it, should be taught.” And Morehead should
be commissioned to expound it, eh ?
Parkes’s last snort :
I think it has been abundantly proved so far that the
present is a worse House for the conduct of business than
any which has preceded it. In other words, I don’t think
that there was ever so much difficulty in the way of the
transaction of business as there is now, One certain
result will be that it will lead to the strengthening of the
Legislative Council. The Upper House will exercise more
influence and will possess a larger measure of power than
it has ever had during its existence. Another result
will he to rouse the inert power which exists in the com-
munity. Take the city of Sydney. There are owners of
the whole of it, and if the owners unite they will possess
more power than those who don’t own anything in it.
Hitherto they have been singularly indolent and neglect-
ful of their interests. What may happen may be a
thorough rousing of this inert power in the community,
a power which has never yet once exercised itself. It
takes a great deal to rouse it, but once roused it will dis-
play a front which has not been seen yet.
This is first, a shriek and a curse at the Labour-
party and an intimation that Property is going to
unite as one money-bag and. wipe it out; and
second, a howl at Property because it hasn’t
united and done the job already. And from
these remarks we can guess how Parkes wants to
spend his old age. He wants to retire to his villa,
with £lO,OOO voted to him by a grateful country,
and from the front window he intends to look out
at the great uprising of the landlords; and then,
in between times, he will write “ poetry ” and
furnish bulletins about the state of his lame leg,
and he will keep a big dog in the yard to scare off
creditors. And now and then, in the twilight, he
will take the infant down to the front gate, and
rest it on top of the gate-post, and there teach the
young idea how to shoot, and lead a simple, pas-
toral existence generally.

soon found himself pitchforked into politics.

Hawkesbury in the N.S.W. Legislature, and

a dozen divisions on one evening.
Henry Halloran and George Thornton are
about the only two left of the residents of Sydney
who subscribed to Sir Henry Parkes’ “Stolen
Moments, published in 1842. A few subscribers
to the “poems ” were acknowledged as “esquires,”
but the bulk of them were “misters.” ’
Mention of Parson Fitchett in a cablegram
reminds one that the Melbourne D.T. comps,
defended themselves against that brainy saint’s
awful hieroglyphics by demanding extra pay for
setting up his “copy.” which was as bad as that
of Kingston, Q.C., of Adelaide, or Francis Myers.

that.

tanned bush bard, is running a volume of short
poems through the London press under the care of
Ijrancis W. Adams. The book named “The

born daughter of the South.”
Rev. Rigg, Wesleyan minister (Vie.) and
President of the Australasian Conference, died
the other day. Ho was one of the few authors of
the Connection, ’ but not a patch, as a literary
man, on his really famous brother—perhaps the
most notable intellect that the Wesleyan Metho-
dists have produced in 50 years.

The personal or family name of the Earl
of Portsmouth is Isaac Wallop. Wallop is
rather a good name for an earl. Yet none of the
earls you meet in the modern society novel are
named Wallop. A few peers named Isaac Wallop
judiciously scattered through the pages of fiction
would do a lot to undermine the British aris-
tocracy. Just think of it! Wallop !

It is cabled that Prince George of Wales
comes to Sydney as Commander with Admiral
Stephenson, Admiral Scott’s successor. By the
way, how unlike Prince George is to the rest of
the Prince of Wales’ family, and how strikingly
he resembles Lord Charles" Beresford ! Placing
their photographs side by side, you might take
them for younger and elder brother.
Kate Reilly, the celebrated coutouriere of
Dover-street, Piccadilly, is married to Major
Griffiths, who, by-the-by, is a cousin of Fred.
Griffiths, of Sydney. The Major is an Inspector
of Prisons, for which he receives £l2OO a year. He
makes at least another thousand by literature, and
as the profits of the dressmaking business are
large, they should have a splendid income between
them.
A back-blocks (N.S.W.) township had an
unorthodox Mayor, who christened his first-born
“Oliver Cromwell.” This jarred the nerves of
the Irish nurse, who said, “ Don’t you know, sor,
that Oliver Cromwell was a very bad man ?—he
cut all the throats of the priests in Ireland !”
“And I hope,” said the happy father, “that if
my son grows up to be a man he’ll cut the throats
of all the bishops.”
The Divine Sara is still spoken of lovingly
by the boots at the Australia Hotel, Sydney. At
11.30 each night she sat down to a sumptuous
dinner; at two she retired ; and at five she re-
hearsed—to the great delight of the boots and
other early risers. The corridor was thronged as
the rehearsal proceeded. Sara never used th e
main entrance when she was leaving for the
theatre in the evening; she invariably went out
by the gate leading into Moore-street from the
basement.
H. B. Marriott Watson’s now novel, “The
Web of the Spider,” is a tale of the Maori war.
Watson is one of the slowly-increasing colony of
Australian litterateurs in London, and, in many
respects, the ablest. He has seen things. Most
of the other men have only heard and read about
’em. Watson owes nothing of his accumulating
celebrity to the cheap—and nasty—“country-
supplement ” publishing firm in Melbourne who
allowed his first remarkable novel to appear in
their collections of hash.
The present Duke of Athole is a combina-
tion of one duke, a marquis, about nine earls, and
fifteen assorted viscounts, barons, and other sun-
dries. It is painful to think that if anybody runs
a tramcar over him there will be 2G peers all lying
dead together in a gory heap in the roadway.
This sort of thing should make an ordinary citizen
feel thankful. If a plain individual named Thom-
son is rushed over by a dray he can’t make more
than one mangled corpse anyhow, which is a great
deal nicer than being 26 dead bodies all at once.
The William Bridger whom we see adver-
tised for in the Maoriland papers, is said to have
come to Australia in the year 1872. Now in that
year, Messrs. Nichols and Lester, hanged at
Darlinghurst for the murder of a man named
Walker, confessed to having murdered another
man named Bridger, whose body was found in the
Parramatta River, at Hen and Chickens Bay.
Bridger had been a steward on board H.M.S.
Rosario, and was decoyed up the river by his
murderers on pretence of finding him a situation.
It was back in the sixties, at a Criminal
Court in the Hunter (N.S.W.) district. It was
always hard for the Crown to get a convicting
jury in a cattle-stealing case, but the evidence
against the prisoner this time was overwhelming
and the Chief Justice (Sir Alfred Stephen) was
determined to have him “ shopped,” summing-up
accordingly. The jury retired for ten minutes
and came back with a unanimous “not guilty.’’
His Honor, after noting the verdict with a
thoughtful air; “Prisoner, you are discharged ; ”
then with a suave smile, “and the jury, too.” ’
The late Mr. Sheridan Moore, who died
in Sydney rather suddenly last week of paralysis,
after an eventful life, was a fine classical scholar
and a man of highly-cultivated literary tastes. He
was_ the tutor of many men who afterwards
attained professional distinction—and he was the
real author of many a flowing oratorical period
palmed off by more than one political humbug as
his (the humbug’s) very own. It was to Moore’s
influence that one or two unlettered people who
have acquired a certain kind of reputation as
public speakers owe their fondness for the ancient
Crakes and Romans. “Whin Dimosthenes
stood by the say-shore ” —how often have we
laughed at it!
When Clement Wragge, the Queensland
meteorologist, was “ at home,” he spent his vaca-
tions in ascending Scottish and Welsh mountains
to collect weather-data. On one occasion, he had
ascended Ben Nevis late one day, for the purpose
of taking daybreak observations next morning.
His friends were surprised at his return the same
evening, and on asking him the reason, he re-
sponded, after a very patent attempt to collect
Ins thoughts, that when he reached the top of the
mountain he had forgotten for what purpose ho
had made the trip. Clement must be the real
original man who absent-mindedly forgot his wife
when he started on his honeymoon tour.
G. R, Dibbs, if not born at the old Plao-
staff Hill, Sydney, at least spent his early days
there. He went to a school kept by A. G.
iaylor s father, or, if he didn’t, he ought to have
done so, as the school was next door to his
mother s house. As a lad (writes a correspondent
who professes to have known George in those days)
he was the leader of a party of boys who used to
make shell grottoes and provide themselves with
‘lollies ” in that way. On great occasions George
playing a fife, used to head his'band and visit all
the houses on the hill in quest of—-well, let us
call it hush-money. When he grew beyond this
kind of thing he was put into a merchant’s office
and afterwards drifted away to Chili, where he
made and lost money.
Mayor M‘Mahon, of Fitzroy (Vic,), who
threatens to c. ntest Best’s seat at next election, is
a tailor who rose to sudden affluence in the land-
boom. But in his earlier days he was a very hard-
driven tailor, and made ends meet with difficulty.
Once Watts, of the Argus (the fellow who started
a first Argos leader with “ If there be a God ”)
gave him an order for a pair of pants. “Very
good, Mr. Watts,” said Mac.; “ those trousers will
be ready be Chusday.” So on “ Chusday ” Watts
called and found M‘Mahon and his only employe,
a tailoress, at work on another job. “ Mr. Watts,”
said the present Mayor, deprecatingly, “I’ve
brukken me word. I have a terrible lot of work
to-day, but I’ll have the pants finished be
ffedmday, Jane (to the girl) put two men on
to Mr. Watts’ trousers at ivonst.” He had to brag
or die, and he bragged.
When Admiral Sir Chas. Hotham assumed
the Victorian Governorship he began to harry the
local civil service. Strolling into Melbourne" Cus-
tom-house one morning, he found the old house-
keeper dusting the place ; no one else had arrived.
The Governor waited until a “swagger’ clerk
appeared, leisurely hung up his hat, and spread
himself out to read the Argus. Hotham
sharply tapped the desk with his cane, whereupon
the languid swell enquired : “Ah—what is —ah—
themattah?” “ Where’s the chief clerk?” “He
—be heah till about half-past ten,”
drawlmgly replied the young potentate, again
hiding his head in the newspaper. “Well, when
he does come tell him that a person known as Sir
Charles Hotham called, and will call again to*
morrow morning at nine o'clock sharp.” And at
nine sharp next morning everyone was on duty,
from the Collector downwards. But presently
the diggers took up the old man’s spare time, and
the civil servants got a rest.

Judge Windeyer is just now weeping over
the fact that there is no place in N.S. W. where
youthful offenders can be sent for reformation.
Yes, there is, Judge—the scaffold at Darlinghurst
gaol. Some 17 or 18-year-olds were reformed there
one fine warm morning not so long ago.
‘‘ ’ Tis never too late to mend ” is an old
saying, very applicable in cases of reformation or
leaving off bad habits, but in cases of sickness or
disease no delay must occur, or it very probably
will soon be “ too late to mend.” At the on-
set of disease almost every case is curable • if
neglected almost every disease will progress
beyond its curable stages. A simple headache is
a reflex sign that some internal organ is
deranged, and this irregularity continues and
increases, although the noticeable signs—head-
ache, &c. ; disappear. An early treatment with
Clements Tonic is necessary, and must, on
account of its scientific composition, prove an
effective remedy. Mrs. Moeller, Georgina-street,
Newtown, N.S.W., writes: “I can with con-
fidence bear witness to the wonderful efficacy of
Clements Tonic in curing liver and kidnev disease.
I have long been a victim to indigestion (caused
by liver complaint) and an affection of the
kidneys. I suffered terribly from headaches,
pains in the back and side, with obstinate
constipation. No one except myself knows what
my sufferings have been. 1 was always tired and
languid, with no ability or inclination to perform
my daily work. My tongue was furred in a
morning, and a sour taste in the mouth, and
frequent vomiting. One day I w r ould be pretty well
and the next very distressed or completely pros-
trated with a very confirmed languor. This
went on until I w 7 as so ill I was completely done
up.and quite unable to move. My ankles being
swollen as thick as my thighs, I consulted
physicians, all of whom pronounced the case one
of dropsy. They variously ascribed the cause to
be heart, liver or Bright’s disease. The kidneys
were undoubtedly diseased, because the swollen
ankles when pressed with the finger would leave a
dent in the place for days, and it was easy to press
the end of the finger an inch deep, when the hole
would not fill up for four days. I had heard of
the wonderful cures effected bv Clements Tonic
and commenced its use. I took three bottles and
the swelling in the ankles commenced gradually to
subside. I continued the medicine, and am now
completely cured ; my ankles are their usual size ;
I have no aches nor pains and can do a good day’s
work with pleasure; my tired aching feelings have
all disappeared, and I am confident I have been
rescued from an untimely grave by the wonderful
virtues of Clements Tonic. My address is at the
head of this letter, and I will reply to any
communications if a stamped envelope is sent with
enquiry, as I consider it my duty to publish a
record of my wonderful cure.” Remember there
are hundreds of imitations in Australasia; insist
on Clements Tonic. ’Tis sold by all chemists and
storekeepers ; to take substitutes is onlv to waste
your time and money.

Governors by popular ballot.

It is said that Sir Henry Parkes positively
refused to appoint Mr. J. Johnson, Member for
Balmain, to the N.S.W. Legislative Council. Mr.
Johnson expressed his wish to several members of
the Parkes Cabinet; but each and all of them
would not hear of a “labour-representative’’being
summoned to the nominee Council to do battle for
the masses against the classes.
Ninian Melville, Chairman of the N.S.W.
Assembly, is very hard to please in chairs. He
has had three chairs made expressly for his com-
fort in about 18 months. He won’t be _ pleased
until he is ensconced in the Speaker’s chair. Mr.
Melville’s chair is not placed on the floor, like any
other chairman’s chair, but on a sort of rotatory
box, which raises him about a foot above the
clerks by his side.

The Parkes’ Ministry rode hard for a fall.
Minister Young openly denounced the party in
the country, and the Premier and the Treasurer
patronised “ General ” Booth. The eight-hour
provision in the Coal Mines Bill was Parkes’ great
card, and in a vigorous speech—the most eloquent
he has delivered for months —he goaded the labor
men into direct opposition, and then and there
threw up the sponge, on the altar of “individual
liberty.”

tinctively “Temperance” Society, if it acts up

A little light is thrown upon the Federa-
tion under the Crown Convention by Hazell’s
Annual. It represents Sir Henry Parkes as one
of the most remarkable men who have risen to
political eminence through journalism. It says,
erroneously, “he visited England in 1887 as the
representative of N.S.W. at the Colonial Confer-
ence which has been described by Lord Rosebery
as the ‘inauguration of Imperial Federation;’”
and “on that subject Sir Henry issued an im-
portant manifesto in October, 1889.
Parkes’ eccentric conduct recalls the
“character” which Dan. O’Connor, his Post-
master-General, gave him not long ago in the
NS W. Assembly :—“ Sir Henry Parkes is a
dangerous leader. He is not fit to be trusted with
power for he is always a tyrant and always a
coward. No man has exhibited a greater desire
to become a dictator than Sir Henry 1 aikes. No
man would more willingly ride roughshod over
our liberties than he. He has sprung from the
ranks of the people, but he is a veritable tyrant.
Said Tim Healy on 20th November last,
three days after the verdict in the Divorce
Court :
We must remember that for Ireland and for Irishmen
Mr. Parnell is less a man than an institution. We have,
under the shadow of his name, secured for Ireland a
power and an authority in the councils of Great Britain
and the world such as we never possessed before. Ana
when I see a demand made tor retirement and resigna-
tion, I ask you to remember the futility thereof. I say it
would be criminal—aye, it would be absurd and foolish
in the highest degree—were we to surrender the great
chief who has led us so far forward.
Parkes played his last cards badly, after
all. He should have retired after the general
election, let the Labour-party squeeze Dibbs, and
come back, if he could, later on, to calmly assert
that he was the only true democrat.
By a majority division of 35 to 15, Premier
Griffith has succeeded in getting his three-legged
Provincial proposals agreed to by Bananaland
Legislative Assembly, the chiefest obstacle thereto
being sponged out by the Northern and Central
members (with two or three exceptions) agreeing
to \\ aive the right of the Provincial Governments
to build up each its own tariff, the argument being
that the newly-provinced colony should be pre-
pared at any time to dovetail with the future
Australian Federation on a basis of intercolonial
Freetrade, with a common foreign tariff. The
next step in the complicated process is to re-
submit the whole scheme in the form of a_ Bill, to
be afterwards approved by the British legislature.
Thus, every stage towards the free self-govern-
ment of Australia is clogged by hampering side-
hobbles to a distant and foreign Crown.
The Tory St. James’ Gazette reluc-
tantly admits that the failure of the Indian
Government prosecution of the vernacular
journal Bangabasi “is a terrible blow at the
Imperial prestige, although the justice of the
decision cannot be questioned.” In other words,
the Gazette is quite prepared to uphold the im-
perial domination at all hazards, irrespective ot
the moral basis of individual acts of administra-
tive authority. Which is preferable—the cool,
smug hypocrisy of the Tory journalist’s position,
or the icy cynicism of the German and French
press, which declare “ We’re going to colonise, and
we’re going to rule the native races who run
against our colonists, and if, in the impact, the
natives go under, well, that’s their look-out ?
Both are brutal, but the latter is at least straight.
Cable item under the heading of “ The
British Race ” :
Mr. J. A. Froude, the historian, says that a federalist
parliament would be an absurdity, while commercial
union is impossible. The bond between Great Britain
and the colonies at present subsisting is not galling, and
it is better not to speak of separation. He supports the
suggestion that there should be periodic contests between
the branches of the British race in industry, athletics,
and culture.
Then the empire is to be held together by high
jumps and consolidated by gymnastics. By-and-
bye it will be linked together by prize fights, and
the “silken bonds” will consist chiefly of damaged
noses and swollen ears. And after that it will
get down, we presume, to rat-hunts, and the State
will be kept from falling asunder by international
rooster-skirmishes. And then it can’t get down
much further, unless it gets down to stamping on
worms.
Dear Bulletin, —Apropos of Parkes’
assertion that the appellation “Riverina” is
excluded from all N.S. Wales Government docu-
ments, the statement has just that proportion of
accuracy which characterises the most of the old
man’s bombastic deliveries from his high horse —
that is to say, it is untrue. I am not going to
waste time by turning up the record, but give
from memory three papers. (1) Cowper’s cor-
x'espondence witli Sir John Young, and that
Governor’s with Downing-street, over the petition
for the severance of Riverina ; (2) Cowper’s
correspondence with the petitioners; and (3)
Morris and Ranken s Land Report. Specific
research would, I am sure, bring out a couple of
’ dozen more instances. The old man should have
consulted on the point his friend and creditor,
Sir Pat. In addition to numerous other reasons
why N.S. Wales people should deplore cna
Riverina was not formed into a separate province
is the certainty that had severance been effected,
Sir Pat. would never have been a N.S. Wale?
legislator.— A Hireling Scribe.
Henniker Heaton states that when he first
went to Berlin he only succeeded in finding in the
great library one book about Australia, and that
was Mudie’s “Felonry of N.S. Wales.” He says
that they have now plenty of Australian boobs,
and sell German-made boomerangs in the streets
at a shilling apiece with full directions in German
and English how to use them.
A deputation of miserable unemployed
men, who waited on the Mayor of Brisbane the
other day. told various tales of desperate willing-
ness to work. One offered to take stone-breaking
at 2s. a day. It was said that numbers of men
could be produced who would be glad to take pick-
and-shovel work at 2s. fid. a day. Atop of all this
destitution the Griffith-M‘llwraith-Tozer trinity
is importing cheap Italian hash, and has lent a
gracious ear to “ General ” Booth's scheme for
importing more cheap and refuse labour.
The winner of the Queen’s Prize at Bisley,
Eng., this year, was David Dear, of whom the
story is told that, being once a guest at a fashion-
able At Home, the servant enquired his name.
“ David Dear,” said he. Penelope Ann blushed
vividly, and aske.d—“ But, please, what is your
other name?” “ I have no other name but David
Dear.” She announced him as “Mr. David.” A
similar occurrence happened to J. Dear, telegraph
station-master at Gin Gin, Q., but long before he
saw that sequestered repeating-office. He gave
“J. W. Dear” as his name, and he was ushered
in by a loud-voiced menial as “ Misthur Jaydub
Belle w.”
The other day a Perth (W.A.) stockbroker
called round at the office of Liberty, and with
premeditated ferocity and malice aforethought he
spanked the arcfiiraedean lever with a large piece
of leather. The injured editor nearly fills the
next issue of the paper with an account of his
feelings and damages. After explaining how the
sharebroker wrote asking him to visit his office,
and how there was 2d. deficient postage on the
letter, he proceeds thus :
We fully intended visiting this Glover in his lair but
the responsibilities of a gigantic undertaking and the
superintendence of a successful newspaper prohibited us
from immediately interviewing this shining light of the
Perth Stock Exchange. On Tuesday afternoon we were
not sitting in our sacred sanctum, surrounded with our
contemporaries, and accompanied by the proverbial
paste pot and scissors, but we were engaged in the
undignified occupation of type-snatching. We were at
peace with the world having had a good week and [a
sufficiency of coin wherewith to purchase the neces-
saries of life and to pay wages. “Click, click, click,”
went the type, as we gradually emptied “stick” after
“stick’’when suddenly our vision was obscured by the
appearance of Glover, who asked for Mr. Anderson. He
was answered in a friendly way and invited in. We
proceeded with our type-snatching when, without the
slightest warning, we were seized by the throat,
virtually garotted, and pinioned to the frame at which we
had been performing honest toil. A long slashing whip
flickered in the air and descended on us with no light
hand. Swish came the whip as the terrible grasp on our
throat tightened and this cowardly miscreant continued
to take advantage of our helpless position. At last just
about the time when we had thought of “ giving up the
ghost” assistance came in the shape of one of our faith-
ful retainers, who seized the infuriated share-broker by
the waist and literally dragged him off his victim. The
exertions of the struggle had evidently proved too much
for our would-be-slayer, his courage evaporated into his
boots, and he lay panting and trembling in our faithful
henchman’s arms, truly a pitiable sight, while we
regained consciousness. Our enemy lay at our mercy,
but his white, wan face, his pathetic gestures touched
our better side, we forgot that he crept on us like unto a
snake, that he as it were, stabbed us in the dark, while
we welcomed him unto our abode. Perhaps we had not
recovered from the strangulation. Anyhow, surrounded
by our principal devils, who had rushed from various
parts of the building and at a sign from the Chief D, our
assailant beat a retreat, evidently glad to get away
so easily from the crowd of jackals, who, armed with
Mannlicher rifles and smokeless powder, at a sign from
their master would have devoured him and his funeral
would have been to-morrow.
MUM.
“ Mum’s ” the word in Russian journals,
Though a million breadless poor
Lie, with “ rats ” in their internals,
Dying at the hovel door;
In the par. or special column
Never shall a groan be heard,
Breaking through the silence solemn —
“ Mum’s ” the word !
Could the monarch and his nobles
If perusing, quite distress’!,
Gruesome yarns of famine troubles,
Eat their plover-eggs with zest ?
Can they save the starving ryot ?
No, the very thought’s absurd !
Better keep the matter quiet,
“Mum’s ” the word.
Let the writers air opinions
On the theme of Russia’s might :
Let ’em howl for more dominions,
Let the trained assassins skite.
Bury deep the rotting masses
Whilst the captains, silver-spurred,
Drink to “ war ! ” in sparkling glasses
(Mumm’s the word).
“ Mum’s ” the word when things unpleasant
Certain critics dare discuss,
Subjects like the Russian peasant
Oft are covered up by us.
Do the Tory journal readers
Find the truth of what occurred
Bluntly told in honest leaders ?
“ Mum’s ” the word !

Society
Another Court scandal in an Australasian
province. This time, it appears, His Nibs has
loved not wisely nor too swell, but stooped to
conquer among the dolly-mops of his country
residence. What “the missus thinks of the
new freak is at present unknown. The former
one cost over £4OOO for the christening.
# * *
Nothing has yet appeared in the papers
concerning the how-d’ye-do at Brisbane Govern-
ment House at the Auxiliary Squadron reception.
It is unlikely that the bleary-eyed M.L. A. who
ran amok on the occasion, ruffling the hair and
smacking the backs of judges and taking the
Admiral unto himself as a long-lost brother, will
again show up on the premises.
* # *
The committee of S.A. bicycle sports
meeting, held in Adelaide on Oct. 10, advertised
that they would absolutely destroy all dogs found
on the Oval. Only one dog made its appearance,
and the committee, with a murderous look in its
eye, captured it, and was about to destroy it by
44 successive pistol-shots, when a committeeman,
with his hair on end, spotted “Government
House” on the dog’s collar. The pistols were
put away, and the dog roamed about quite con-
tentedly, nearly bringing down the riders several
times. That’s all.
* * *
Lady Hamilton is contributing to a Tas-
manian paper some articles on “ public health.
The introductory article is a sorry specimen of
English composition. Many a lad is whipped for
showing so lamentable an ignorance of Liindley
Murray.
* * •
It is cabled that the London Times “ fears
that some of the best Australian wines are some-
times introduced as French wines, as entertainers
would scarcely venture to place Australian and
Cape wines before their guests.” This is likely
enough. Some years ago a member of the JN.&. W.
Agricultural Society, who was entrusted with the
catering of liquor for an association banquet, as a
practical joke denuded of their labels six or seven
dozen bottles of high-priced German hock a,nd
attached them to similar bottles of Australian
wine (costing 16s. per dozen) from the Hunter
River. The learned connoisseurs of the Agricul-
tural Society drank the wine, smacked their lips,
and were quite astonished to learn that such
excellent Rhine wine could be bought as low as
60s. per dozen. A similar trick was played on
Lord Norman by when'he governed Victoria.
The old man arose at the festive board, glass in
hand, and lovingly holding up to the light a bum-
per of Trouette and Blampied’s white wine (which
he accepted as from Germany), and said a day
migftt come when such a vintage would be gathered
in this fair land. (Loud cheers.)
# * *
A Sunnyside (N.S. VY.)State-school teacher
having been convicted of criminally assaulting a
young female pupil, and sentenced to eight years’
penal servitude, it is pointed out that three male
teachers have locally been convicted within a very
short space of time, and the suggestion is made
that only women should be allowed to take charge
of mixed schools. The Bulletin suggests that
ere this step be taken as a specific remedy the
records of the Victorian Education Department
in such matters be attentively studied.
» • •
Wicked as Victorian secularism is, N.S. W.
beats it in seductions by schoolmasters.
The death of Jules reminds a
Bulletin French correspondent of the scandals
with which his son-in-law, Daniel Wilson, was
connected. “The sale of decorations has long
been a fruitful source of income to certain politi-
cians in Paris. Soudry, who was shot by Greer,
In the Theatre Royal,' Melbourne, was connected
with a regular business of this kind. He and his
partner had sumptuous offices in a small street off
the Rue 4re Septembre, where they drove a
thriving trade. Michel Ephrussi, the Jew
banker, paid them 14,000 francs for a ribbon of
the legion of honour, and after the esclandre he
fave another 14,000 to have his name kept dark,
t leaked out notwithstanding, and Soudry
et Cie were convicted and sentenced to long terms
of imprisonment. Soudry told me that he was
merely the servant of certain Deputies, who used
their political influence to procure decorations
for anyone who could pay sufficiently well for
them. Soudry bears the mark of Greer’s bullet,
which injured some of the muscles of the neck,
and produced an involuntary bobbing of the head,
which reminds one of those small Chinese figures
which wobble their heads and put out their
tongues when moved.”
* * *
“Bob” Prendergast, the rich Sydney
brewer in whom the auri sacra fames burns
strongly ever since he missed buying a share in
Mount Morgan, has found in England a veritable
diviner who can infallibly indicate where gold h.es
concealed. She is a very charming young lady,
the daughter of a clergyman, and Bob met her at
a country house. Miss discovered her power
by mere accident. She was at some place in the
North where a water-diviner was at work, and
being much struck with what she saw, she got one
of the divining twigs and tried her hand. To her
astonishment it acted at once, and since then she
has frequently thus found water. On the occasion
when Prendergast met her he asked if she had
ever experimented with gold, and when she replied
in the negative he arranged to test her power.
He had gold with him, sovereigns or nuggets, and
secretly deposited some in several places. The
lady took her rod, and after wandering about for
some time, was able to mark the spot where each
piece of gold lay hidden. Everybody who saw it
was astounded. The test was perfectly bond fide,
and there was no suspicion of collusion. So much
impressed was Mr. Prendergast by the exhibition
of the lady’s mysterious power that he has made
her a tempting offer to visit Australia with a view
of utilising her skill in searching for the precious
metal. This is a true bill.

naturally modest, they found themselves the un-
invited guests of a picnic-party of the elite of Bre-

* * *
Melbourne Metropolitan Liedertafel has
now become the “ Royal ” Liedertafel. Apropos,
N.S.W. Agricultural Society is, or was, the
“ Royal ” Agricultural Society, and how it became
so was that, without asking anybody, the members
agreed to call it “Royal,” and Royal it was
accordingly.
# * *
A week or two back an aged party, placed
in Melbourne City Police Court dock “ under the
Yag.,” gave his age as 75, and at the same time
described himself as a “ Waterloo veteran,” thus
showing himself to have been a full-blown soldier
a year before he was born. The guileless Panton,
P.'M., swallowed the yarn, and gravely remarked
that “ something should be done ” for the suffer-
ing hero. We would suggest, for the guidance of
magistrates, and also as a hint to those blithering
old idiots wbo wish to pose as Wellington’s own,
that 00 years of age is about as low as a Water-
loo) an can now go. Why, very shortly, a Soudan
Contingent veteran will have to be at least 30 to
be genuine.
# * *
A strange story is going round concerning
a certain gubernatorial establishment somewhere
in Australia. There were two or more daughters
in it —the number of daughters doesn’t matter,
but one of them took music-lessons which were
supplied by a ponderous German professor.
Another, a younger one, was a little, kittenish
creature who often sat in an al fresco manner on
the stairs, and relapsed into day-dreams. She
was there one day when the professor came
down. She was dreaming and he was near-
sighted, so he fell over her with a yell and a crash,
snatched at her hair and her ears to save himself,
missed them and turned a somersault, and fled
wildly head-first into the hall. The Governor —a
real live Governor made of meat —looked out
from a door somewhere on hearing the din, and
the spectacle he saw paralysed him. There was
his daughter sitting on the stairs; and camping in
a dishevelled state in the hall was the professor
bowling at her in a whole variety of foreign
languages. Being shaken violently by the collar
and asked to account for his coarse expressions
the unfortunate man explained that he had fallen
over that horrid dog on the stairs. Being in-
formed that it wasn’t a dog but a young lady, he
found his lost eye glass and looked with a long,
wild look, and then with a shriek of horror he
burst out at the front door and fled for his life.
It is presumed that he has committed suicide ; for
a man who falls over and then swears at a
viceroy’s daughter there is no refuge except in
death.
# # *
A coolness has arisen between the ladies
who run the Austin (Vic.) Hospital and the local
bicycle club. The bicycle club for some time past
has got up a reputation for benevolence because it
used to come round in all the glory of its calves,
and its exaggerated breast-bone, and sing and
play to the patients. But after a while it began
to be asserted that the club really came round to
hug the nurses, and that it used to be found on
moonlight nights outside the nurses’ windows
singing love-songs, with a banjo and guitar, and
breathing forth its soul in harmony. Other
rumours have it that the club’s idea of benevolence
was to lay for some consumptive who seemed to
need cheering up, and bellow “rorty” nigger
songs in his last moments. Anyhow, the hospital
seems to hint that if the comes round
on its errand of mercy any more, it will whoop
for the police in short order and have these good
Samaritans thrown out of the front gate.
# * *
It has just transpired that New South
Wales subsidises the capture of deserters from
tbe Imperial navy by a reward of £5 for each
man caught. In other words, when a seaman,
tired of his dog’s life in a service which he entered
as a mere infant, clears out, goes to work, saves
a few pounds and gets married, the N.S.W.
Government pays out of the public treasury the
infernal sneak who drags the sailor-man away
from his wife and children, preparatory to his
being lagged for years in an English prison!
What does the democratic party intend do'ng in
this matter for the protection of Australian
women who may marry British naval absconders ?
* * *
One of the biggest attempts at political
organisation yet made in the Australias has been
initiated by a Southern association, who are
whipping-up their constituents to the tune of
20,000 circulars a week. The circulars are printed
at a Union office, but this concession is accounted
for by the consideration that the highest and
mightiest plutocrat in the organisation is the un
recognised boss of the printing-concern. The
envelopes in which the circulars are sent out are,
however, addressed by typists at the rate of ss.
per 1000 addresses. Now, a decent remuneration
for that class of work when performed on the
typewriter is 14s. or 15s. per 1000 envelopes. This
little circumstance will just show_ what the
Unionists may expect if the association becomes
top dog,
* * *
And, by the way, The Bulletin is glad
to hear that the Sydney typists are proposing to
unite to secure better remuneration, a higher uni-
form standard of qualification, and a general
“ elevation of the profession.” A Typists’ Union
will have the best wishes and support of The
Bulletin if it gives a cold shoulder to those
machine importers who are mainly responsible for
the market being flooded with scores of incom-
petent and cut-tbroat operators. And another
thing the Union will have to do will be to choke
once and for all the favouritism manifested by
certain Government departments towards some
typewriting concerns, which are bringing the
sweating methods of the factory and the laundry
into the ranks of a superior—heaven help it! —
profession.
# * *
A well-known recently-divorced Sydney
man is going to console himself with another wife
—gilt-edged.
Mr. Ardill, the professional philanthro-
pist, tells us that in Sydney there are 2000 fallen
women under police surveillance, “ besides many
hundreds of others who give their evenings to
vice.” Now, accepting Mr. Ardill’s first figures
as correct, the world’s police experience show’s
that “ hundreds ” in the latter part of the sentence
quoted should read “ thousands.”
* # *
It will interest a lot of people to learn that
the popular Mr. Reginald Pell was married at
Broken Hill on 7th inst. to Miss Harris. It was
rather cruel of Reginald to thus flaunt his happi-
ness in the faces of the Gaiety Co. He might
have postponed the happy event until after their
departure from Australia.
* # #
The Grand Orient of France has been
formally inaugurated in Sydney and has come to
stay. The motto of this order of Masonry (which
is regarded as a damnable concern by the other
Freemasons) is “Liberty, equality, and frater-
nity.” Doesn’t bow down to Tummy or grovel
to anyone merely because he is “ royal ” or '■ich ;
and is, in short, a democratic society.
# # *
The hysterical Pastor Grubb, expounder
of the new East Lynne religion, says that no
clergyman should drink or smoke. “ Let any of
you Christians,” he adds, “ put a pipe of tobacco
and a glass of whisky before you and see if you
can ask the blessing of the Lord upon it.” Grubb
might as well say let any woman put a pot of tea
before her and see if she can ask God’s blessing
on that. Tea does far more harm in the land to
women than tobacco does to men. It turns ten
per cent of the women into querulous old hags.
# * *
Odd rule by Victorian Football Associa-
tion: No convicted criminal is to be allowed to
play.
* * *
Mr. Frank Suttor has brought in a Bill in
the N.S.W. Assembly to enable any married
couple to legitimise a child born out of wedlock.
The Bill will be regarded by more than one
eminent politician as beastly personal.
# # •
The Hobart girls are idolising the Auxi-
liary Squadron. A meeting of the citizens,
convened to organise a welcome to the officers,
rejected an amendment that it would be “ unwise
and immoral ” to honour a body of men “whose
only occupation is to destroy life and property in
the interests of the so-called upper classes.” The
leaven of democracy is spreading. Ten years ago
no one would have dared to submit such a motion
to any Tasmanian assemblage.
* * *

titled and untitled, at the wedding (at the parish
church, Old Windsor) of Rev. J. H. Hmdsqn,
M.A., vicar of Wraysbury, Bucks, and Miss
Lavinia Butler, daughter of Hon. Robert Butler.
The rev. bridegroom is an Australian, being the
son of Captain Hindson, who for many years
lived in Lower Fort-street, Sydney. The old
gentleman was one of the few lucky persons who
bought land in Melbourne in the early days and
kept it. His family consequently are very well
off, indeed. “Lorry” Hindson is well known in
LOVE OUTLASTETH ALL.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Could I borrow the laverock’s lilting note,
Or the silvery song from the blackbird’s throat,
Then would I warble the whole day long,
Telling, in floods of passionate song,
How worlds might tremble, or skies might fall.
But Love, true Love, outlasteth all.
Or, with picturesque words, in phrases neat,
With ringing rhymes, and in sonnets sweet,
Had I the skill of the schoolman’s craft
My song the murmurous breeze should waft,
And tell to her whom my heart loves best,
How Love outlasteth all the rest.

A young lady who hunts with the Devon
and Somerset stag hounds rides straddlewise on a
real Australian buck-jumping saddle which she
had specially made for her at Wangaratta. At
present she has not found anyone to copy her, but
no doubt cela viendra.

# * *
An Australian girl not long ago married in
England a clerk in the Herald’s office. Three
weeks after marriage the bridegroom became
insolvent, and among his various debts was one of
£lB5 for bouquets supplied to ladies whose names
and addresses he had utterly forgotten.
Is this a typical young Australian ? Blessed
if we know, only a D. Christie Murray could tell
us. It was a youngster of 13, and he was bitten
with the stamp-collecting mania, and he swapped
a Royal Humane Society’s bronze medal which he
had received for saving (with his clothes on)
another kiddy from drowning, for three Italian
postage-stamps.
* * »
A Melbourne business man, who hails from
Sydney, takes pride in (modestly) indicating to his
occasional guests his Soudan and Egypt medals !
“Member of the Contingent, y’ know ! ” Which
he just wasn’t. He got the medals for the price
of two long beers, cashed on the spot.
# * #
The Bulletin is glad to find that
Bruce Smith has the good tmste and wise
intelligence to authorise the printing of the
N.S.W. Public Library’s catalogue of its Aus-
tralian collection. Although this work has been
prepared primarily for the convenience of the
Library staff and frequenters, it will form the
most important contribution to the bibliography
of these provinces yet published. When issued to
the public no person interested, either as a student,
a journalist, or a public mfm, in the history and
development of the Australias, can afford to be
without it. Its compilation lias been literally a
labour of love to Mr. R. C. Walker, the Librarian,
whose name it will keep green for many gene-
rations.

* * #
The Prisoners at Pentridge are, naturally
enough, dissatisfied at the recently-inaugurated
regulation abolishing meal-muster, and providing
that each man shall eat Ids meals in solitude, and
threaten revenge. There is no doubt that the
innovation adds 50 per cent, to the severity of the
punishment, and will be most detrimental to the
health of the men, especially those undergoing
long sentences. Think of a man being locked up
for from twelve to fourteen hours nightly, debarred
from all conversation during the day-time, and
having to eat his meals in his foetid sleeping-room
m summer!
* * •
Some few years ago the R.C. Bishop of
Maitland had a high old time dining with thirteen
females—that is to say, the dinner-party consisted
ot fifteen, and thirteen of them were females,
and the other two were the Bishop and a clerical
friend of his. It was a painful situation for a
reverend father to be in. He had gone to Merriwa
to hold a Confirmation-service, and while there he
thought it would be the appropriate thing to
invite the committee and its wife to a hospitable
P res bytery. He meant it kindly, but
toe committeemen felt scared. None of them had
ever dined except in a rugged fashion at the
mtchen-table; they were accustomed to whack
the offspring in the middle of the meal, and
to reach for the victuals with their paws, and they
fli kn°w how to dine witli tiie reverend
j *?er. Therefore, unknown to each other, seven
Qt them were called away to' look for a lost cow,
and six had to go kangaroo-hunting, and thirteen
lone females rolled up to explain the situation.
The six women whose husbands were kangarooing
got on the spot first, and when the distracted
bishop was through with them there set in a
steady procession of six more whose husbands
were all away looking for a cow. Then there was
a pause, and the troubled father began to make
bets with himself a,s to what animal the remaining
committee-mam might be out pursuing, but just
then the thirteenth woman appeared coming
down the street, and her partner proved to be
after a cow also.

# # *
A clergyman travelling on the Brisbane-
Gympie line the other day dropped his pocket-
book in a railway-carriage. A lad picked it up,
and after some time spent in making inquiries,
the parson got it. “Is it all right ?” asked the
lad. “Yes, there was the sum of £9O, and it’s
exact, thank you. And, now, here’s half-a-orown
for yourself—not for your honesty, for honesty is
its own reward, but for the trouble to which you’ve
been put to find the owner.” The parson was
from Aberdeen awa !
» * *
Delaney, a Hobart ex-detective was
“ beaked ” the other day on a charge of prodding a
man in the eye with a pole, or something, for accus-
ing him of disguising himself and “ working off ”
Arthur Cooley, the Richmond murderer. Case
dismissed on the ground of extreme provocation.
Delaney will be best remembered by old blue-
jackets as the man who earned many a bright
pound of their hard-earned savings in “rewards”
for running them to earth in the shortest possible
space of time after they had overstayed their
leave.
# * *
According to the Victorian law a barrister
who has studied Victorian law in Victoria is pre-
sumed to know less about it than a barrister who
has never been in Victoria at all and never seen a
local Act. An English or Irish or Scotch barrister
is by statute held qualified to be a Victorian
Supreme Court Judge five years after his “call.”
A Victorian barrister is not qualified till he has
been practising eight years.
# * *
A Parisian firm of private detectives an-
nounces its terms for procuring the necessary
evidence in divorce-cases. They have a uniform
charge of 500 francs for securing the conviction of
a suspected wife, or 900 francs if they supply the
lover. It recalls to mind a little incident they tell
about a case— which, by the way, was patched up
out of Court —in one of the Australian capitals. A
very festive gentleman in high official circles, who
had made a love-match with a somewhat mature,
but (financially) charming, widow, was unfor-
tunate enough to awaken the suspicions of his
affectionate spouse. She had an interview with a
detective, and. with a lavish disregard of expense,
bespoke his closest surveillance. Less than a
fortnight later a cab brought the detective to her
door glowing with the pride of success. Madame
drove off with him, and in a few minutes, after a
little scuffle at a door in street, was confront-
ing her delinquent husband and her rival, a
fascinating little minx who seemed to look upon
the surprise as rather a joke. It was too much
for the injured lawful wife. With brandished
claw she sprang to the assault, and the “creature”
was in imminent danger of sustaining much
damage to her meretricious beauty, when the
detective interfered, and, pulling back the in-
furiated matron, excitedly whispered : “For
goodness sake don’t hurt her ! That’s my wife !”
* « #
Millicent, Marchioness of Stafford, is a
pretty little woman who did Australia with her
hubby five years ago in a trip round the world,
and then wrote a book about it, which she called
“ How I spent my twentieth year.” It is dedi-
cated to “ those who so hospitably entertained
and kindly instructed us,”and shows that the lady
spent her year in making cruel sketches of her
entertainers and instructors, and writing cruel
things about them. The illustrations are the
Marchioness’s own. After inspecting them, we
quite understand the following entry
I took sketches of S., Lord Carington, Francis, Mr.
Dailey, Lord Dudley, Mr. Moncrieffe, Mr. Williams, Mr.
Astley, and Mr. Sheriffe. Lady Carinyton had to go up
to Moss Vale to see the children.
Lady Carington had seen her guest’s sketches
before. The interest of the volume centres in the
patches of biography and personal description.
This sort of thing ;
Sir Alfred Stephen is an old gentleman of nearly
ninety, with charming manners and a wonderful
memory, with over seventy vrandohildren and great-
grandchildren. . . . Sir Thomas MTlvvraith, who is
interesting as being the first to annex New Guinea. . .
Lord Carington has a staff of very happy, very lazy, and
very pleasant young gentlemen. ... A big dinner
was given last night in honour of ... a gentleman
with a handsome wife and very tall daughters. . . .
Mr. Gillies, the Premier of Victoria, is rather an interest-
ing man. We had an animated discussion about fanatics,
and both agreed we liked them, and wished there were a
few more in the world, but from rather different points of
view.
On December 18 is a reference to a terrible case
going on in Sydney, in which six young men were
condemned to be hanged :
Lord Carington, who had to sit upon the Executive
Council all day, was much upset by it.
By what? The next evening the Government
House party sang part songs, and Lord Carington
accompanied them on the flute. Why has nobody
ever told Hop. all these years that Lord Carington
played the flute?

Here is a curious little item :
Jan. 26, Government House, Bombay.—ln the early
freshness of the morning we accompanied Lady Reay for
a ride by the sea-shore. ... I was mounted on a
nice little Arab; but as my saddle was too small and my
solar topee too large, I didn’t enjoy myself particularly.
Her ladyship certainly has a most embarrassing
way of taking the public into her confidence.
That sort of thing is all very well in the intimacy
of a diary, but when it comes to publication one
would have thought it sufficient to say the saddle
was too small without going into further parti-
culars.

PIEBALD RELIGION.
At the Methodist Conference recently held
in New York, LT.S. A., the “coloured ” clergymen
were not permitted to patronise the same restaur-
ant as the white parsons.—(Yews Item.)
White and black at the Mercy-Seat;
But they draw the line at meat.
Will they draw a colour-zone,
When they face the “Great White Throne? '
Will they want two Thrones, alack,
One Throne white, and t’other black ?
Deacon Philip was “one of the seven”
First appointed as guides to Heaven.
Deacon Philip (as we’re advised
In the Parsons’ Book) one day baptized
A nigger eunuch of Ethiop.
(Enter Modern Parson .)
“ But they didn’t feed at the same hash-shop."
A BUSH TRAGEDY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Fast in the terrible grip of the waste of a Western plain,
’Nealh the shade of a drooping wilga—overhead
Already th’ accursed crows were turning and wheeling
again,
And our search through the tangled mass of the scrub
had been in vain
For we found him—dead !
Stretched on the ground with his face upturned to th
sky he lay,
One hand as in agony clenched across his breast.
Who shall carry the tale to his wife, and what shall he
say?
How shall he easiest break the new s of this terrible day—
Of the end of our guest ?
Awful the stare of his sightless eyes, and the pose of his
frame
Loosely flung out as downwards in death he had sunk ;
Here came a deep-chested roar from our leader. Long Jim
was his name:
“Dead I—well, may I be hanged, but he 7t«« just played
us a game.
Dead ! !
Why the blessed man’s DRUNK !

STILL IN THE LEAD.
The great remedy for pain, St. Jacobs Oil, is a
wonder surely. But it is the result only of true
medical science and research. Such a discovery
had a right to prominence. It has cured wherever
used as directed. Founded upon the intrinsic
merit of each element, which, in combination,
proved to be the perfection of cure, its virtue has
made it what it is.
THE PEOPLE
of the United States know its name, and name it
as a remedy which should be in every house, for
every home know r s its value, as a sterling
comforter, the truest relief,
THE WORLD OWNS IT.
Canada took it up and adopted it as a standard
cure. It crossed the ocean, on demand, and alike
in Scotland, England, and Ireland, in the extremes
of climate and temperature, it is the same
sovereign Conqueror of Pain. Distinguished men
and physicians of South and Central America,
have used and recommended it, and sti'eet-criers
have sung its praise in verse in the streets of
Mexico. A member of Parliament
IN SOUTH AFRICA
praised it in open debate, and the same was done
by a U.S. Senator. In India it won its first gold
medal at the Calcutta Exposition, where expert
chemists and physicians awarded it its due; and
by merit it has won in all similar competitions.
One of the most distinguished scholars of Germany
thought it his duty to praise it, and such has been
its meed from Prelates, Potentates, and the Press.
A distinguished traveller comments on its use in
Egypt. It was Tire comforter and cure of the lost,
half-frOzen Arctic e'xplorefs of the Greeley expedi-
tion.
BRITISH SOLDIERS AND SAILORS.
It found its place in the knapsacks of the British
soldiers in the Soudan, and its permanent supply
to the medicine chests of her Majesty’s warships.
This would seem enough to show what it is
doing; but one other potent fact can’t be dis-
regarded. It holds its own as the best known
remedy of the age for rheumatism, neuralgia,
lumbago, sciatica, and all painful ailments. It
has taken its place in the trade of this continent,
and has received from many of its numerous
patrons throughout these colonies, as elsewhere,
statements that cures made by its healing virtues,
from the date of cure to the date of response,
embracing a lapse of years, have remained per-
manent without recurrence.

A POPULAR MISTAKE.
Many people have an idea that the Waterbary,
because a cheap watch, is therefore cheaply made.
A visit to the company’s factory, at Waterbary,
Conn., would decidedly alter this impression. On
seeing the extent and variety of the machinery
used, and the neatness, order, and organisation
there exhibited, you begin to realise that making,
a cheap watch (and a good one) is not by any
means the off-hand affair you had thought it was.
As you pass through department after department
and observe the working of wonderful automatic
machines, which make parts so fine you can
scarcely see them, and yet so accurate that they
can be taken at random and fitted together to
form a perfect watch ; as you note the handling of
the parts by dainty and skilful fingers, the
delicacy of the, process, and the thoroughness and
care as to every detail, you find it difficult to
imagine that they are not making hundred-doJlar
watches ; and you then become convinced that the
Waterbary is cheap, not because poorly made, but
because unique and simple in construction.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A “reliable” consultation yarn. A N.&.W.
man who used to live in pauper circumstances
with his family in a hut, drew the winning ticket in a
recent big consultation, and is now fooling away his
“ rise ” at the rate of £300 a week. He has bought 100
tickets in a Melbourne Cup sweep and is wagering odds
that he will win the boss prize, lie gave £50 towards a
local day’s races, and has distinguished himself by buying
three horses, all readied up, clipped, &c., for the occasion,
and all shicers - most of the local horses being able to run
rings round them ; and he is now wildly backing one of
his mounts to win the big handicap, he actually laying
the odds of £100 to £5. He has bought a hay-and-corn
store, into which he has installed as manager a circus-
clown whose show recently visited his locality. He is con-
stantly buying horses and turning them out to grass,
often never seeing them again. One horse is said to have
been sold to him three times already. He is constantly
surrounded by bloodsucking spielers.
# * *
That Nat. Gould (“ Yerax ”) should be able to
write an average sporting yarn was only to be expected,
but why on earth he should have written “A Double
Event” under the belief that Hawley Smart was a safe
guide, when so much peculiarly Australian and indi-
genous material w r as waiting to be dished up in a pecu-
liarly Australian and indigenous manner is a puzzle only
to be solved by the consideration that “Yerax’s” early
training impressed him with the conviction that Hinglish
sport, as Hinglish everything else, is a model for all
creation. Perhaps it is so, which will account entirely
for the blackguardism which, in spite of “Verax’s”
forced argument to the contrary, has pretty well
omnipotent sway over the Australasian turf. In
“A Double Event,” “Yerax” trots out the old
familiar Hawley-Smartian heroes and villains—the
foolish lord, the intellectual bookie, and the extremely
beautiful bookie’s daughter, who, in the social circle of
Don Juan house, unconsciously developes the unvarnished
grace of the best “ home”-bred girl, and the fascinating
elder lady with a page that mustn’t be read in the first
volume of her life’s diary—oh, we’ve had ’em before, a
hundred times! Also, the self-sacrificing trainer and the
jockey with a conscience—will anybody give us his
address?—and the amiabledetective who, somehow’, is never
to be run against in real life—it’s always the unamiable
one who runs against us. Yes, all the dear, old familiar
faces “ Yerax” paints for us, only instead of Epsom and
Ascot he gives us Randw’ick and Flemington, and he doesn’t
talk of Frith’s railway station, but only of Redfern and
Spencer-street, and instead of the Derby and the Oaks, we
get the Melbourne Cup, and in lieu of Lord Falmouth we
get the Hon. James White and Donald Wallace, and for a
change from Bend’Or and Hermit are favoured with Car-
bine and Musket and other of the equine heroes whose
pedigrees the rising Australian youth knows a great deal
better than he does the Lord’s Prayer. Now, the clever
little writer could give us a genuine Australian yarn if he
would kick himself free of imported hobbles. When he
does so, he will exclude the Peer of Hingland, who is
rarely virtuous except when he is an idiot; also the lady
with the mysterious page in her interior, who goes out
into the shrubberies at 11 o’clock on moonlight nights in
evening dress to sit down on mossy banks for the pur-
pose of preparing the bouquets for next morning’s break-
fast. plates and of being ruthlessly and quite unnecessarily
slaughtered by the sanguinary villain whom she had
thrown over when a great, pure love awoke in her heart
for the British Peer aforesaid—and so forth. “Yerax”
knows the Australian turfite as few know him, and, there-
fore, let him set to work and describe him as he is, not as
he would look, dressed as an English cad, in an imported
looking-glass. Further, when Nat. writes his next book,
let him make up his mind as to what he is going to call it,
and not send it into the world with three different titles.
And, finally, is it fair to the editor of the doubly-DEAD
Bird to call an English squire after him ?

The skin of Musket, Carbine’s sire, in Dunedin
Exhibition, reminded a visitor of the skeleton of Sir
Hercules, so long preserved in the Sydney Museum.

A daily paper correspondent suggests that “ in
order to protect the public from the blackmailing pro-
pensities of horse-owners as now practised ” sweeps should
be drawn on the day after the race.
* * *
Near Dunkeld, Vic., the foxes that infest the
country are all married and bring up large families. At
Devon Park, a vixen was killed in the midst of her progeny
of eight, and on the Nooroondoo estate one was captured
with ten babies “ en mite."
* * *
As “ dead-birds ” are in fashion just now it
may be interesting to hear of one a Carlton boniface got
recently for a Melbourne suburban meet. Three ponies—
Lucius, Good Iron, and Fly, ran in one event, and—
“Don’t fail, old man, to put something on Lucius.”
Boniface didn’t forget, and Lucius carried his fiver at
evens. Good Iron won, but as the judge was out of his
box assisting the stewards in a squabble the race had to
be run again. Boniface shook hands with himself at his
let-off, borrowed £10, and put it on Good Iron, again at
evens. Besides, he remembered then that this same pony
had beaten a field of horses over the sticks but recently.
The race was this time won by Fly, the two other animals
having baked themselves with their efforts in the first
race, in which Fly ran nowhere !
* # #
Few Australians will be sorry to hear that
Ferris is a frost as a bowler this season. Not that any-
body objects to J. J. himself, for he is a good little chap,
but the dodging to work his qualification for the
“Graces’county’ disgusted every true lover of cricket.
He played for South v Noi th at Scarborough, and though
the wicket was made for him he could do nothing com-
pared with George Lohmann who took five wickets for
twenty-five runs. Murdoch, Ferris and Woods are all
playing for the “ Gentlemen of England ” at Scarborough.
This savours of farce, for though Ferris may possibly
have a qualification, it cannot surely be said that
Murdoch and Woods are bona fide entitled to be styled
as English gentlemen. Spofforth is clean out of it.

In a certain big Caulfield Cup sweep, the
numbers that “drew' horses” W’ere;—8260, 8262, 8263,
8264, 8265, 8266, 8269, 8270, and 8271. What does this
arrangement suggest? A unique chance in the law’ of
probabilities—almost as uncommon as the dealing out of
13 trumps in whist ? Or ?
# * #
At Rosehill the other day a mare owned by a
well-known metropolitan trainer fell, but luckily the
youngster on her back w as not hurt. When he returned
to the paddock, he received practical sympathy from the
trainer in the shape of a “ stern reminder” that horses he
rides should not fall, although the young’un has not yet
solved the problem as to how he is to keep them on their
feet. But one better comes from Bananaland. A wealthy
Queenslander owned a horse which fell in a race, and
severely hurt the rider. A bystander suggested that a
cab be sent for to take the poor fellow away, but Mr.
Owner looked quite hurt himself as he remarked that he
had already dropped enough money on the blanked
animal.
Carbine, at the moment of going to press, was
quoted at 200 to 1 for the Melbourne Cup. From which
we assume that either the old veteran moke is in a very
bad way, or that Donald Wallace is in a very good one.
The choice lies between the two. Possibly Carbine is
going to break all his baekers ; possibly again, Donald
Wallace is taking up all the money in Israel and is going
to break the bookmakers. It may be Carbine’s leg that
is swelled, or it may be his owner’s head. Anyhow,
Donald W. has been honest up to date.

A piece of Crown land in Pall Mall,
London, was recently leased at a rental based
upon a calculation that the capital value of the
site was half-a-million sterling per acre. This is
illustrative of the sinful enormity of any nation
parting with its earth possessions, which acquire
a value due solely to national progress and not to
individual enterprise.
The recently - deceased Australasian
Critic, a microscopic magazine edited by a large
variety of Melbourne University professors,
abounded in such gems as “consequently, there-
fore,” and “facts is what we want.” The Critic
would certainly have lived had not the cold-
hearted printer declined to accept “Odes to
Sappho”and “Symposia on the different means
of familiarising Australian bushrangers with the
Greek digamma ” in payment of his bills.
The West Australian, a daily journal
published in Perth, is now permanently enlarged
to four pages. This is the paper which Governor
Broome dubbed the “saving influence.” He
meant it, too, for the servile manner in which it
backed up Broome in his embroglio episodes was
remarkable. The “saving influence” surely is
departing from its “ respectable conduct ”
(Hackett’s favourite boast) when it publishes in
its issue of Sep. 23 nearly a column of Bulletin
matter unacknowledged.
An unconscionable amount of lying (writes
our correspondent) has been perpetrated about the
abortive attempt to float a loan of a million and
a half for New South Wales privately in London.
Notwithstanding denials, it is perfectly true that
attempts were made to engineer such a loan ; but
the thing was clumsily managed and failed.
Three well-known men in the city, one of them a
member of the firm of Mullens and Marshall, the
brokers of the Bank of England, undertook to
work the oracle, and they went to a few of the
biggest jobbers “ in the House,’’ who subscribed
a portion of the loan, some £50,000, some more at
a low price—97, ’tis said. But somehow the little
game leaked out, and the jobbers who were left
in the cold—some of them biggish men—took huff
and determined to “crab” the business, and suc-
ceeded so well that the project had to be aban-
doned. The whole story is an open secret in the
city, and forms a standing joke in the parlours of
colonial banks. Of course several good stories are
told of how the conspirators, when they thought
or knew the game was up, tried to “ have ” each
other, and one of them “ sold against ” his own
allotment. Of course this was found out, and
there are “ wigs on the green” between some of
the big firms. The fiasco has given unmitigated
pleasure to certain big people (who must be
nameless) connected with the colonial banks,
for they have put their heads together to
smash up any new Australian loan unless
it be carried out under their auspices. A
big N.S.W. conversion loan is inevitable, and it
will certainly be a matter of extreme difficulty to
borrow any large sum of money in England in the
present state of affairs. The ancient state of
things has passed away, never to return. It was
a good time for some people—old Westgarth, for
example. That leary old bird saw clearly that as
money became cheaper in England, and as the
colonies and their resources became better known
the time would arrive when colonial credit would
stand high in esteem. He accordingly bought any
amount of colonial stocks and subscribed for new
loans (with a syndicate behind him, of course) at
such a price that they were enabled to borrow
money on the stock and pay for it, whilst they
were drawing bigger interest by far than they
were paying. That is to say, they were able to
borrow from 2 to 3J per cent., while the bonds
were bought at a price which gave them in many
cases as much as 2 per cent, margin. If I
mistake not many of those who are now most
active in opposing colonial loans were members
of the syndicate which carried on this very profit-
able game. Time went on and at last the general
body of the investing public began to nibble at
colonial securities, the price went up many points
per cent, and the clever speculators cleared out at
a big profit, and realised enormous fortunes in
many instances. At last it became unprofitable,
and the business was dropped. Westgarth retired
from business a rich man, and within 12 months
of his death his firm was shaky. The game of
these old hands is plain—to decoy colonial loans,
and to run down colonial credit. The late shearers’
strike in Queensland, and the Labour victory in.
N.S.W. are the two stalking-horses which they
are working to alarm investors, and so far the
game has been successful enough. But when the
inevitable happens, when needy Treasurers are at
their wits’ end for money, and the necessity of con-
verting old loans, or paying them off—which will
be impossible—arrives ; when colonial credit.is at
its lowest, then will come again the tilth of the
successful engineers of Australian finance, and
large fortunes will once more be made.

Photographer Anaschentz of Lissa has
succeeded in making twenty-four separate pic-
tures, each showing half-tone and detail, of a
dog in the act of jumping over a small bush,
In some districts of West Australia, if a
blackfellow finds that during his absence his
better half has been misbehaving herself with
somebody else, and, should the case against the
defendant be clear, plaintiff and co-respondent
immediately disrobe, providing they have any-
thing to doff, and each man talces turn-about in
stabbing his neighbour with his spear. After one
has had as much as he can conveniently stahd he
cries go. Whereupon the victor, whether plain-
tiff or co-respondent, has to hand himself over to
the vanquished man’s brother or uncle and, with-
out retaliating, calmly receive from this in-
dividual enough stabs to equalise matters.
The latest W.A. gold-discovery is respon-
sible for some alarming local symptoms of
excitement. Several of the original prospectors
of this new find are reported to be piling tip the
gold in sacks. The locality of the alleged new
discovery, says a Bulletin' correspondent, is in
the Murchison district, about 300 miles from
Geraldton, and is a region practically waterless
and almost barren. The new field, strange to say,
.is not alluvial, and is confined to a small reefing
area, the stone from the surface being phenomen-
ally rich. Whether these reefs will go to any
depth is an open question, but experts think they
will pinch out immediately sinking is proceeded
with in earnest. The stuff obtained is “ specimen
gold ” not in the least water-worn, The few
who have the monopoly of the line of reef are
certainly in possession of large quantities of
“ dollied ” treasure. The two discoverers of
the field are reported, on apparently re-
liable testimony, to have “ no less than
£15,000 worth of the precious metal in their
possession! ” But it is unanimously agreed
that a rush to the spot at the present time
is little short of insanity. Already 500 men are
on the ground, only a dozen or so of whom are on
gold. The Government have just proclaimed the
field, and are sending out a warden.
The feast which has just concluded the
days of Atonement is observed nowhere more
religiously than in Darlinghurst Gaol, where the
Jew is the envy of the Gentile even as he is out-
side. A creed which inculcates good feeding
recommends itself to the strict observance of its
adherents far better than one which prescribes
maceration and abstinence. Sardines, salmon,
lobster, and herrings pour in wholesale to the
order of the chosen, and when such a phenomenon
as a destitute or impecunious Jew is met with
these kippered aids to devotion are supplied by
the Synagogue. After the Gentiles are yarded,
mustered, and celled, Israel is formed into pro-
cession, and with their bags of “ tommy ” slung
across their shoulders are marshalled into the
building used for their services. There they enjoy
themselves to their hearts’ content until as
late as 1T30 p.m., gorging themselves with these
unaccustomed delicacies and passing votes of
thanks to Moses. Then they are drafted off to
their respective wings, the opening of each
“peter ’’ awakening the smothered curses of hap-
less Christian prisoners who went to sleep on
hominy and dry bread, and who at this witching
hour experience an aching void and an unutter-
able yearning. There is a good deal of stern, un-
flinching eating enjoined in the law of Moses, and
if the old pebbles knew how to pass the rabbinical
scrutiny there would be many defections from the
One True Church. In addition to their gustatory
privileges, the children of Israel get the Christian
holidays as well as their own, they are appor-
tioned the lightest work, and their sentences are
fictitious.
The Right Hon. Viscount Monck, P.C.,
&c., &c., by his representatives, distrained for
rent the other day on a Sydney widow.
A prisoner at Darlinghurst occupying the
high and responsible position of barber, which is
an office of profit under the Crown, inasmuch as
he gets a special indulgence in the shape of
tobacco, (just as the N.S.W. Attorney-General
used to get fees plus salary,) was very recently in
trouble for having, ill concert with other “ retired
civilians,” committed a most brutal assault Upon
the son of an ex-Minister of the Crown who was
yarded with him. The tonsorial artist first
knocked his man down, then three or four con
federates closed in, and before any warder could
interfere, his principal assailant, who is in quod
for a precisely similar offence, gave him a brutal
kick in the eye, splitting the lid, and impairing
the sight. The victim was discharged the other
day after serving the full term, subject to good
behaviour. What was done with the barber, no
one knows or ever will know, save the suave and
coldblooded Minister of Justice. He may have
been flogged, or he is to be flogged, but the public
knows absolutely nothing of this subterranean
administration of alleged justice, that ought to be
dealt out in open daylight.
Dear Bulletin,— The N.S.W. Betterment
system taxes land-owners proportionately to the
good done them t that is most fair. The revenue-
raising ruse, adopted in clause 24 of the N.S.W.
Public Works Act, taxes those whose lands are
resumed in proportion to the good done all other
people . That is unfair and stupid. Let me illus-
trate : —A and B own two adjoining quarter-acre
allotments, worth, before the trouble, £40 each.
These allotments are equal in all things. The
railway takes away half A’s and does not touch
B’s allotment. B, of course, does not want com-
pensation, and A wants it, but does not get it.
Government says to A i “You lose £20 worth of
ground, but the piece left to you is enhanced
100 per cent.; that leaves you uninjured.” B’s lot
is now, on the same basis of enhancement, worth
£80. So, really, is not A taxed in proportion to
B’s benefit and not in proportion to his own? The
extraordinary consequences end not here. A re-
cognises that the half of a building allotment is of
no use to build on, and so it is practically lost to
him, though the Government arithmetic says it
ought to be as valuable as the quarter-acre was at
first. Please remember that no lots were ever
purchased here for the purpose of exacting an
exorbitant or any price from Government. I can
furnish oceans of proof of the bona-fides of these
lot-holders. Listen : B owned a lot on which was
a house. Government took one-third of his lot
and the house, and offered £3 15s. compensation.
After six months of application and refusals, after
throwing the rest of his lot on the local market in
vain ; after working M.L.A.’s and solicitors he at
last learned that he was awarded £108 ; the valuer
had taken him for someone else. In many cases
85 per cent, profit was made to the owners by the
liberal compensation awards. That makes it
worse for the others. Same thine re broad acres.
D h as 20 acres 11 miles from CoroWa; 10 acres are
under best muscat vines. The railway, cutting
the area obliquely, takes three acres out of it and
adds 100 per cent, to the cost of working the vine-
yard. The Government officers say the working
of the vineyard should he done by spade. They
also say some day the property will be cut up into
town lots and so be enhanced. Yes, when Bargo
Brush become Sydney town-lots.— Cobowa.
