Tales of the Old Regime.
No XV.—The Ross Gang “Varner"-ship,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Chummy, yer’ll have to pitch us some-
think !”
“ ’Ear. ’ear!” came in a strident, ironical
chorus from the bed-places.
“ Hi, parson, stir yer pins !”
“ Hooray for parson ! Give us some preach in’,
old Devildodger!”
“ No, tip us a yarn, but sarmints be blowed !”
“ Aye, aye, Chummy, no sermon, but give us a
song.”
“ Vy, ’e can’t sing nothink but ‘ Hold Hun-
derd,’ and ‘ ’Vake my soul an’ with the sun.”
“ Well, old White Choker, yer’ll have to give
us something, quick, or we’ll make you run the
ranks ! You got to do something to pay your
footin’, if you won’t give us ‘ Franky.’”
“ Verily, dear Brother Snufflebuster, unless
thou art moved to speak quickly these thy
brethren in the System will be obliged to ad-
monish thee, and to inflict upon that mass of cor-
ruption, thy sinful flesh, the chastening hand of
affliction !”
“What, no answer? The right reverend
refuses to speak, pals ! What shall we do to
him? ’
“ It ’im hon ’is ’ead !”
“ Stand him on his head !”
“ Douse him!”
“ Make him dance the bayonets ! You’ve only
been out three months, and must have brought
the latest dance with you, parson, of course,
from home —home, sweet home !”
“ Sling him to the rafters !”
“ Rank him!”
“ ’Ear, ’ear ! Rank him, aye, rank him !”
And “ Rank him ! ’ had it. Even the advocates
of the bayonet-dance, popular though it was, were
less numerous than those who cried for the
“ranking” of the Reverend Arthur Athroyd,
M.A., sometime curate of All Hallows’, London,
who now stood in the middle of the dormitory of
Ross Probation Gang. And the vote being for
“ ranking,” instant preparations for the pastime
were set on foot.
# # *
Let us go back three mouths—to the time
of the Rev. Mr. Athroyd’s arrival.
When a newly-arrived prisoner was added to a
probation gang he was required to pay for the
honour and pleasure of being included in so choice
an assortment of scoundrelism. “Close” cor-
porations always exact premiums for admission
to the enjoyment of their privileges and per-
quisites, and that exquisitely-beautiful appliance
invented by the great Earl of Derby for eradicating
humane instincts from the convict nature, which
he called a probation-gang, formed a society
whose members were bound by the closest of all
ties, those created by a mutually-dependent vil-
lainy. The membership of most privileged bodies
is knit together by common interests of profit. To
that cementing power were added in the case of
a probation-gang the tremendous attraction of
mutual fear and mutual distrust—of an embittering
sense of wrong inflicted by a common enemy, the
System—of an incessant “study of revenge”
against the forces which directed the energies of
their foe—of a common hatred against all things
and all men—of a knowledge shared in common of
practices the vilest and crimes so scorching in
their horror that the very ink would dry upon the
pen that attempted to describe their true char-
acter. There was thus peculiarly good reason why
every new Probation Ganger should pay his
entrance-fee upon joining so select a circle. The
deeper the scoundrelism which dyed his co-mem-
bers, the more necessary it was that he should
qualify himself by the payment of “chummage”
or “garnish.” They would have to trust him so
much that it was only just and proper he should
trust them with as much as he could. They
placed their necks, so to speak, in his hands, and
by all principles of equity it was right that they
should have their feet on his neck. Great com-
fort is derivable from the consideration that if a
man can hang you, you can also hang him. It
precludes treachery for one thing—and the men of
the Regime always encouraged treachery and paid
pretty well for it.
Now, the Rev. Athroyd, M. A. (Oxon.), having
made the journey of 80 miles or so from Hobart
Town to Ross on foot and on the chain, and
having been the recipient at Oatlands and two or
three other “ stop-over depdts ” of delicate atten-
tions from the escort of soldiers and Field-police
which had left him shirtless, sockless, cashless,
hatless, and all but bootless —the corporal of the
guard who had discovered that Athroyd’s new
London-made Wellingtons were his own exact
measure, had generously presented him, in ex-
change, with a dilapidated, unpaired couple of
“ration’’-shoes—this ex-clergyman, we say, was
not in a position to pay any “footing” at all
upon his introduction into the Probation Gang
at Ross. The fact was almost at once made
apparent to the elder members —some seventy in
number —who looked with interest and excite-
ment towards the door as, half-an-hour after
“ lock-up ” one December evening, the ex-clergy-
man was thrust into the domitory, and they lost
no time in resenting it.
The cries of “New boy!” which greeted him,
had not died away before the voices melted into :
“A new Miss Nancy! Mary Jane! .Hurrah!”
and the echoes of these again, were still ringing
when the hawkings and vulture-cries changed into
a howl of disappointment.
“He won’t do ! Make him pay footing, then !”
And “Footing! Chummage! Garnish!” con-
tinued to resound through the rudely-slabbed
building till half-a-dozen of the more energetic of
the gang, who had “ run through ” the garments
of the miserable new-comer, were convinced that
he had not the worth upon his person of anything
negotiable to the value of a single “bit.”*
“Not a bit’s wuth on ’im, stow my lights!”
cried the gang-bully, captain by right of supreme
blackguardism and brutish strength. “Take
that, you mug, fur lettin’ ’em clear yer out before
yer got into the company of us gen’i’men ! ”
And a blow, which split lips upon whose
eloquence some year a-gone had hung the eager
attention of titled dames and damsels, followed
the words. It was the precursor of other blows.
And who shall say they were not deserved ? What
right had a new man to import himself into the
privileges and immunities of Ross Probation Gang
without paying the customary toll in person, in
coin, or in some negotiable article’ What right,
indeed!"
'• # •
The Rev. tMr. Athroyd. however, recoiled
from tfie blows—and moaned. Moaned, and then
uttered his first words.
“ Why, why do you treat me in this way ?
What have I done?’’
“ You’re not a Mary Jane!” answered the bully.
“You’re not a Miss Nancy !” shouted another.
“ You’ve no tin—nothing to pay your footin’!”
cried others.
“That’s not my fault!’’ the creature who had so
mightily offended the susceptibilities of the
inhabitants of the dormitory ventured to remon-
strate, in a whimper. “ I've been robbed.”
“Wot right ha’ yer ter be robbed?” demanded
the bully—Dalton—as he aimed another blow at
Athroyd, who evaded it.
“How could I ” The new-comer had got
thus far in self-exculpation when Dalton struck
him again—this time with force sufficient to draw
blood from the nostrils.
“ ’Ow could yer ? Wot’s that to us ? An’ 'ow
dare yer dodge my shot for yer peepers ? Take
this, an’ this, an’ don’t be a coward! Us don’t
’low no one as is cowards ’ere, do us, boys ?”
The manliness of this sentiment naturally com-
mended itself to the feelings of several other
gangers, who emphasised it so strongly and pun-
geritly that the “ new boy” dropped to the floor in
a semi-swoon. Then, and then only, was there a
lull in the cordial demonstrations which had wel-
comed him. For ten minutes or a-quarter of an
hour they permitted him to lie undisturbed, save
by his own tremors. At last he rose dazedly, and
stood or rather reeled in the centre of the room.
Already the bruises were showing on his features,
and his eyelids were swelling ominously over
watery eyes. The gang, half afraid that it had
gone too far, regained its vivacious temper and
its voice as they saw him struggle to his feet.
The bully re-opened the attack.
“ Now, ’ow his yer a-goin’ to pay yer shot?”
The ex-clergyman answered :
“ I’ll—pr-pray—for you 1”
* * #
The resulting roar of laughter was of such
volume that it brought the sergeant of the guard
to the peep-hole in the door. The merriment was
so genuine that, of course, it could not be allowed.
Only the ring of hearty laughter would have in-
duced the officer to emerge from the guard-room
snuggery. Cries of “Murder!” had more than
once burst after nightfall from the dormitorv, but
an enquiry into their origin had been postponed
till the morning. Laughter, however—genuine,
unforced laughter—was a very different thing.
The slide was well oiled—by virtue of a special
regulation—and it turned on its pivot noiselessly,
so the sergeant’s tones were the first intimation of
his presence.
“To your bed-places there—or I’ll fire!” he
shouted, and the gangers skurried to their lairs.
All except the “new boy.” His wits had barely
come back to him, and he was necessarily not yet
aware that a sergeant of the guard was a very im-
portant personage indeed, and entitled to be
obeyed on the instant. He retained his place.
“ To your bed-place there, No. 72, or I fire !”
The Reverend Mr. Athroyd knew he was “72.”
To that extent he was familiar with the System.
But as to a bed-place, none had been allotted to
him, and how could he go to one unless
His speculations were summarily terminated by
the passage of a musket-bullet through the calf of
his right leg. The Probation Plan was responsible
for many innovations into the System, but it did
not affect in the least the good old principles
which led the authorities to immediately resent
overt or covert disobedience. Once again the ex-
clergyman dropped to the floor.
It might have been the making of him
that trifling casualty—as Superintendent Argus
of the Ross Gang styled it in his monthly report
to the Comptroller-General For it kept him to
his bed-place for ten weeks ol so, notwithstanding
the many attempts of the Superintendent to get
him out to work. l( We don’t allow shamming
Abraham, parson !” that genial gentleman had
himself informed him. “Damn it, sir, under
Captain Wright at Norfolk Island I’d have had
you to work the very next week, had you pro-
voked a shot by insubordination there. But we’ve
got milk-and-watery now, thank your stars ! I’ve
a good mind to try you as it is. How’d you like
to work in trumpeter irons, parson ? Quite a kid-
glove touch about ’em, I can assure you—it would
remind you of the days when you pressed the
hands of London ladies !” But in spite of his
pleasant words, Superintendent Argus thought it
best to permit the “ parson ” to regain health and
strength—good spirits he could not —before put-
ting him to work. And the months which might
have been months of acute torture were only the
measurings of so much dull, dead pain.
The gang left him undisturbed by their refined
attentions for the time—although they were still
determined on compelling him to pay his footing.
In the day-time he was alone in the ward, save for
a chronic hospital case—a youngster who was
asthmatically puffing his life away—and frequent
“ transient” patients who were in for a day or two
while backs were going through the saline aijd salu-
tary process of healing. And in the night-time, he
was free of the gang also. Only that his spirit
quailed before the nameless horrors of the others'
nights, he would have been tolerably comfortable.
The transport, who was officially appointed
wardsman, and officially recognised as “yarner ”
to the gang, was won over to kindness by per-
mission to drink Athroyd’s ration of rum. As a
patient on spoon diet, two ounces of rum was
allowed Athroyd daily. Which Wardsman Twiss
not ungratefully absorbed.
“ Spiritu’s drinks ain’t good for yer, pa’son,
they’ll indoooe inflammashum.”
“ Perhaps so!”
“ And jest yer guv up yer grog, an’ I’ll put yer
up to gettin’ on purty well wi’ tn’ boys.”
Athroyd shuddered. God ! Had he, a gentle-
man, a scholar, fallen so low, as to feel the neces-
sity of getting on well with the “ boys !” But he
said nothing, and Wardsman Twiss did not
apparently notice his silence.
“ I’m due for a pass to work nex’ Monday
se’en nights, an’ th’ gang’ll want a yarner, w’en I
goes !”
“ Yes?” Athroyd felt an impulse of interest in
spite of himself, as he thought that perhaps,
perhaps, he might till the yarnership! Better a
thousand times the yarnership, with its disgusting
menial duties inside the dormitory, than work
outside in the hourly degradation of companion-
ship with “ the boys.”
“ Yes, that’s wot I think. Yer not a bad ’un,
pa’son, on’y a fool, an’ if yer guv up yer grog ”
“ Yes, yes, you can have it!”
“Didn’t I call yer a fool? Wot d’yer say I
c’u’d ha’ it fer till yer seed wot I wud swop for it ?
Yer are a fool.”
“ I trust you, you see !” said Athroyd.
“ Then, thet jist proves as yer’s a double-fool!
To trust a ganger 1 Well, thet’s rich. Ha! ha!”
“ But what —about the yarnership?”
“ W’y sh’u’dn’t yer take it? Yer know « lot
o’ yarns, an’ tales, an’ romances, don’t yer?” / v
“ I could tell some—many, in fact !” *
“Then w’en I’m gone, I’ll nom’nate yer—jest
out o’ pure likin’ for ver, pa Von. An’ there ain’t

* » *
Athroyd, steeped in the misery of his
stupor though he had been, had yet during his ten
weeks’residence in the gang-dormitory, perceived
that the yarnership, accompanied as it was by the
wardsmanship, was the most coveted of the billets
open to the transports. The nomination of their
own “yarner” was about the only opportunity
possible to the gangers of exercising their faculties
of choice and judgment, and was a privilege only
granted to them in order that the man whom they
selected might be bound by force of self-interest
to the Authorities. It always suited the Authori-
ties to so arrange that at least one man in every
gang should be in close touch with the prisoners,
on the one hand, and with themselves on the other.
By attaching the wardsmanship, therefore, to the
yarnership, and by filling the latter honourable
post on the nomination o the gang, The Authori-
ties generally killed two birds with the one stone
-they pleased, at no expense to the Government,
the prisoners ; and they, obtained for another a
ready, pliable tool possessing the intimacy w.th the
convmt nature so essential to the proper working
of the system.
* ' *
True to his promise—for he had nothing
to gain by breaking it—Wardraan Twiss began
that very day the work of preparing the way for
the assumption of the yarnership by the “Par-
son.” He had two things to do—one to instruct
Athroyd in the best means of gaining the atten-
tion of his audience, and the other to win the
suffrages of the gangers for his protege, in case
there should beany other aspirant for the position,
“ Wot d’yer know, pa’soh ? Tell us some yarn,
an’ I’ll see how yer’ll get on wi’ th’ boys ! ” he said
one morning just after “ gang to labour ” had rung
from the station bell.
Athroyd thought for a moment—ransacked his
memory for some stirring story—and could think
of nothing better than some poor weak narrative
of the mawkish order, with a moral in it, and a
good boy who became a clergyman, and a bad
man who was hanged, and so forth.
Twiss interrupted him after five minutes,
“ Didn’t I call yer a fool? To tell a yarn o’thet
kind to our boys ! Are yer looney, man ?”
Athroyd tried again. A battle story from one
of the histories this time, and he earned a little
more commendation.
“Thet’s better, but, lor’, one o’ those ’ll be
’nuff for a week, an’if th’boys want yer, yer’ve
to find a new ’un every night. Yer never knows
when they’ll make yer strike up.”
Athroyd’s heart sank. He was a University
man, but his culture was at fault here. His loads
of learned lumber did not qualify him to interest
a convict audience.
“Don’t yer know any murders, or hangin’s?
They’re alius ready fer ’em?”
No, Athroyd knew.next to none. His acquain-
tance with the Newgate Calendar was with its
cover only.
His tutor was disappointed. “I’ll ha’ to guv
yer up ! I can’t nom’nate a cove wot’s no good—
th’ boys ’ud never forguv me! Stay ” A
thought struck him. “Ha’ yer a purty good
mem’ry? Can yer git a thing by heart, quick-
like ?”
Athroyd thought he could. He would try, at
least, he said. . .
“ Then I don’t mind a-puttm’ yer up to I rank
the Poet’s Tower.’ They’ll take th’ Tower, th’
boys ’ll, mornin’, noon, an’ night. But don’t yer
guv it too frequent. I guvs it four times a year
—Easter-day, an’ Christmas-day, w’en we gets
the duff, an’ per’aps twice ’sides. But the boys ’ll
take it offner, and never get sick. A varse at a
time, and then one of yer own yarns, an’ the varse
’ll help ’em to bear t’other patient-like. Or guv
’em yer rubbage fust, and then the Tower.”
“ What is the Tower?” questioned the ex-clergy-
man. “ I never heard of it.”
“It’s Frank the Poet’s travel’s in ’Ell!”
“ Oh, ‘ tour ’ you mean !”
“ It’s his Tower I say, an’ if yer don’t like it any
way, yer shan’t ha’it at all,” rejoined the offended
treasury of Frank’s precious verses. “ Yer’ll say
Tower, or I’ll not put yer up to it.”
And Athroyd could only make his peace by
consenting to substitute Twiss’ pronunciation for
his own.
* * •
On the Sunday before Twiss would lay
down the honours and emoluments of office, he
had determined to test the feeling of the gang
towards the,appointment of Athroyd, who, by
this time, by dint of much thought and applica-
tion, had contrived to memorise his version of
Twiss’ version of Frank the Poet’s famous “ Tour
through Hell.” Twiss waited till after dinner to
mention the matter, well knowing that he would
be called upon for a yarn to drive away the som-
nolence induced by that specially luxurious meal.
The gang were always sleepy on Sunday after-
noon. The alteration in their ration from 12
ounces of meat, boiled to a soup, to 16 solid
ounces (including bone) of “roast,” naturally
suggested the afternoon nap of happier and freer
Sundays, and therefore the System insisted that
they should not go to sleep. If they did not road
the Bible, or “ a good book,” they might listen to
a yarn, but go to sleep they should not.
Well, the gang did insist. “ Bully ” Dalton
called out for a yarn. “ An’ it’s yer las’ Sunday,
ol’ pal—so chuck us ‘ Franky.’ ”
“ ’ear, ’ear, ‘ Franky’s Tower !” was the sincere
chorus. Tower—for the last time,
Twissy !”
Twiss took his station at the end of the dormi-
tory—the hospital end—and held up his hand for
silence. The congregation at St. David’s Cathe-
dral in the capital that afternoon were not more
devout.
“Now, I wants to tell yer, pals, that I’ve a
per —posishun to make !”
“ Never mind thet. Give us ‘Franky !”
“ If yer don’t want a Darlin’ necklacef ’n yer,
yer’U hold yer tongue, my flash cove !” threatened
the yarner to the interrupter. “My per—
posishun is to do Franky if yer’d shet up!”
Half-a-dozen blows were aimed at the disreputable
fellow who had interfered with Mr. Twiss’ plans,
and Mr. Twiss went on.
“ My per-posishun is this, that as I’ve to leave
yer, dear boys, yer’d better guv my billet to th’
man ’mong yer who knows all ‘Franky’s Tower!’”
There was a burst of “ Hear, hears ! ” and then
—a dead silence. On second thoughts, who was
there among them all who knew the “Tour”
except the man who was about to resign ?
“ Yer don’t know enyone, don’t yer? ” Twiss,
rejoicing at the effect of his words—he really had
a sort of regard for that poor weakling, the parson,
—went on. “Now, I does know one of yer as does
know the Tower off by ’art! But will yer ’lect
him?”
“ Yes, yes ! ” roared from three score voices.
“Then, pa’son—pa’son Athroyd, stand ycr
forth ! ”
And, leaning upon a rude crutch which Twiss
had made him from a broom-handle, the ex-clergy-
man limped into the middle of the room. He
held his hand to his heart—the spasm of shame
which seized him at being thus the object of
distinction to an infamous crew, had nigh stopped
its beating.
# # #
The surprised gangers were as good as
their word. They elected him unanimously to the
yarnership as soon as he had demonstrated his
familiarity with the convict-classic, and generously
forgave him his “footing.” He recited to them
the introduction only that day, however. “He
shan’t guv it yer all at once, genTmen,” quoth
his patron, “an’ye’ll ha’to’sense his narvusness
this ’ere day, ’cos that’s only natural w’en a cove’s
blushin’ honors are thick upon him.” An admir-
able and magnanimous sentiment that was rap-
turously applauded.
The portion he recited that day, taking care-
how the trifling concession stung him ! —to suit
Twiss’ views in the matter of the pronunciation of
“Tour,” was this ;
You prisoners all of New South Wales,
Who frequent watch-houses and gaols,
A story now to you I’ll tell—
’Tis of a Poet’s Tour to Hell.
On the highway before he died
Our hero’s valour oft was tried ;
At length to Death he fell a prey—
To him it proved a happy day.
Downward he bent his course, we’re told,
Like one destined for Satan’s fold,
And no refreshment would he take
Till he approached the Stygian lake,
And thinking no one would molest him
He stood—when Charon thus address’d him.
“Stranger! Art thou friend or foe?
Thy business, too, pray let me know.”
“ Kind sir ! I come from Sydney Gaol.
My name I mean not to conceal;
And, since you desire to know it,
On Earth I was called “ Frank the Poet.”
“ Are you that person?” Charon cried.
“ I’ll ferry you to yonder side,
And, stranger ! do not troubled be—
I’ll grant to you a passage free !
A fee of sixpence is my charge
. t For the passage in my barge ;
But, noble guest! I’d' have you know it,
I never mean to charge a poet.”
This canto—and the next, in which the Poet
. . . Leaving Charon at the ferry,
Went direct to Purgatory.
he recited that first day. Beyond that he would
not gratify them. And when he left off, they were
eager to hear more.
# * #
“ They’ve made you Yarner !” exclaimed
Super. Argus next day. “ Made a parson
yarner ! Lord, what a joke !” He paused to have
his laugh out. “But you can have the wardsman-
ship, vice Twiss, due for pass—on one condition,
No. 72.”
“ Yes, sir! ”
“ That you don’t preach to them and make ’em
too saintly.! ” And he went away to tell the
subaltern of the guard—Ensign Chick—that his
superlative scoundrels had chosen a parson for
their yarner.
“Jupiter, he’ll give ’em the Acts of the
Apostles some day, Chick ! ”
* ' # *
But, nothing of the sort.
. Most of the gang had heard the “ Tower ” half-
a-dozen times from Twiss, but Twiss had never
known but a garbled version of the poem. He had
himself received it orally from a convict as
illiterate as himself, and the clever verses had
become prettily mangled in transition. Athroyd’s
scholarship, however, had enabled him to restore
the text, and the gang welcomed his rendering as
though it were altogether novel to them.
During the first day of his appointment as
wardsman, he carried in his recital Frank to the
very Gulf of Hell, and told his hearers how
Frank —
Having found the gloomy gate
Loudly knocked to know his fate.
Then fonder knocked —and louder still.
Till Satan came, “ Pray what’s your will ?"
“ Alas !” said Frank, “ I’ve come to dwell
With you—and share your fate in Hell! ”
Cried Satan: “ That can’t be! I’m sure
For I detest and hate the poor.
And none shall in my Kingdom stand
Except the wealthy and the grand.
And, Frank, I think you’ve got astray
For convicts never come this way !
But soar to Heaven, in droves and legions.
A place, so called, in the upper regions.
And, Frank, I think, with an empty purse,
You might go further and fare worse ! ”
How they cheered at this point!—it was after
tea on the Tuesday—and how they bent forward,
intent to lose no word of the Poet’s response to the
Chief Ruler of the System :
“ Well,” cried the Poet, “ since ’tis so,
One thing from you I’d wish to know ;
As I’m at present in no hurry—
Have you one here called Captain Murray ?’’
“ Yes ! Murray’s here ! Within this place ;
Perhaps you’d like to see his face ?”
“May God forbid that I should view him ;
Aboard the Phoenix hulk I knew him.
But who is that in yonder blaze,
On fire and brimstone seems to graze?”
“He ? Captain Logan, of Moreton Bay ;
And Williams —killed the other day.
He was overseer at Grose’s Farm,
And did you convicts no little harm !
Cook, who discovered New South Wales,
And he who first invented gaols
Are together tied to a fiery stake
That stands in yonder boiling lake.
Hark ! Hear you not that dreadful yelling ?
It conies from Dr. Warden’s dwelling.
And yonder see those fiery chairs,
They’re fitted up for beaks and mayors.
And” men of judicial orders,
Barristers—lawyers and recorders.
And how they made him repeat the next canto,
twice and thrice :
Then Frank beheld legions of traitors,
Hangmen, gaolers and flagellators,
Commandants, constables and spies,
Informers, and overseers likewise.
In flames of brimstone they were toiling,
And lakes of sulphur round them boiling !
Hell resounded with hideous yelling
Alas ! How dismal was their dwelling.
Then Major Morriset he espied
With Capt. Clunie by his side,
In a fiery belt they were lashed together
As tight as sole to upper leather !
Their situation here was horrid -
They both were tyrants to the Nor’ard.
Next he beheld old Sergeant Flood
In Vulcan’s hottest forge he stood.
He gazed on Frank—his eyes with ire
Appeared like burning coals of fire 1
He by a bloody collaps'd band
Was to a lofty lamp post chained.
With fiery garments all arrayed,
Like a wild Arabian ass he brayed,
Loudly he implored from Frank assistance
To end for him his sad existence.
“ Cheer up !” said Frank, “ don’t be dismayed,
Remember No. 3 Stockade.”
Twice and thrice they made him recite that, and

547 George Street (war Liverpool su, Sydney.
they encored him as fervently when, on the Wed-
nesday, he related how, to Hell’s door,
Drove up post haste a coach and four,
And about six feet of mortal sin
Without leave or license, tumbled in.
And how—
At its arrival three cheers were given
That reached, at least, the highest heaven
And all the inhabitants of Hell
With one accord rang the great bell
That never was known to sound or ring
Since Judas sold our Heavenly King.
Drums were beating, flags were hoisting,
Never before was such rejoicing—
Dancing, singing, joy and mirth,
In heaven above, or on the earth.
Straightway to Lucifer Frank went
To learn what these rejoicings meant ?
“ Of sense,” cried Satan, “ I’m deprived
Since Governor Darling has arrived !
Fire and Brimstone I’ve ordained him,
Vulcan has already chained him.
But their pleasure at the tortures heaped upon
poor Darling was feeble, compared with their
delight when Frank dealt out to his and their
class that happiness which the plain prose of life
refused the convict. When he recorded the fiat
that
Although in convict costume drest,
Heaven hailed Frank as a welcome guest;
and described the welcome in detail, they laughed
and cheered so as to bring the Sergeant, the
Ensign, and the Super, to the peep-hole. Nor in
their delight did they notice that their orator
could not force himself to utter—the conventions
of his profession still holding him closely—the
irreverent lines: —
Enoch ! Go with him to Job—
And put on him a silken robe.
St. Paul! Go to the flock straightway
And kill a calf or two to-day,
And tell Abraham and Abel
In all haste to prepare the table—
They heaved deep sighs of regret because, after
Frank had shaken hands with many saints from
foreign lands; and after the curfew had rung
“through Heaven’s Concave,” and “hymns of
praise had been loudly sung after the Poet had
thus “glorified his theme,” that
He should awake and find it all A Dream.
* * #
That grieved them.
They enjoyed the tour as every generation of
convicts enjoyed it —enjoyed it, because it wafted
into their nostrils the sweet incense of a revenge
which would be all the sweeter because they would
relish it from the Heaven that seemed so distant
from the regions controlled by the men of the
Regime.
# # #
But—Frank’s Tour was only a dream, and
Super. Argus was a reality. And when Super.
Argus discovered (though the spy-hole) what was
the main burden of the Rev. Mr. Athroyd’s
recitals, there was a row.
“ Don’t you know, sir, that that is a grossly-
insubordinate effusion ? ”
Athroyd pleaded that he had not so regarded it,
but his pleas were swept aside as they deserved to
be.
“To-morrow morning you’ll be tried and
you’re removed from the wardsmanship, d’ye hear.
You’ll be lucky if I let you off with thirty-six and
the stone-heap.”
That was on the Saturday night of his first
week in office.
« # *
On the Sunday—the day being kept holy
as per regulation—the Reverend Mr. Athroyd
received an illustration of his luck—36.
And on the Monday, he received a further
illustration of it by being sent to the stone-heap.
On the next Sunday, no other yarner having
been appointed—that billet had been kept open,
by common consent, though a new wardsman had
been placed in charge—the gang called upon him
to repeat the “ Tour,”
“ Yer not in a billet now 1 ” cried Bully Dalton.
“ Ah’ yer’ve been catted wunst, an’ arter th’
wunst it don’t matter ! ”
Athroyd bad manhood enough left to shake
his head.
Then the gang treated him as we have told in
our opening paragraph. He had not paid his
footing, they said.
To rank a man was to make him “run the
gauntlet.” Limping, he was forced up and down
the doubled ranks while they struck him and spat
upon him. Athroyd had been whipped—and he
was such a fool that, having been once in official
favour, he could not keep in it—and therefore he
was a thing to be trodden upon —kicked—outraged.
But there was this consolation for him —it was
not the first nor the last Sunday afternoon spent
similarly by the Ross Probation Gang. The Ross
Gangers often used to keep themselves awake on
Sunday afternoons by “ ranking ” a new-comer.
The more refined the new-comer, the more delight-
ful the pleasure.
PRICE WARUNG.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*
A San Francisco pressman summed up
the Jackson-Corbett scrap in one sentence :
“ Yesterday Jackson was the coloured champion
of the world. To-day he is only a nigger.”
THEY ROLLED ON THE FLOOR.
On Monday, November 24th, 1890, the American
papers published the following news item :
“Mrs. Sarah S. Hensler, of No. 873, East One-
Hundred-and-Thirty-fourth-street, New York,
shot and killed herself yesterday morning. She
was a lady of excellent character and high social
position, and a member of the Rev. Dr. Ramsay’s
.Presbyterian Church. She was well-to-do and
very active in various public and private charities.
Since last July she had suffered fearfully from
indigestion and dyspepsia, which brought on
melancholia, and then a kind of insanity, under )
the influence of which she took her own life.”
Here is another story, not so tragical, but with
the same moral. The narrator tells it of himself.
“Mostly,” he says, “we dread and fear death,
yet once I prayed to die, and the reason was in
this wise. Up to Christmas, 1888, I had been a
healthy man, but at that time (a period of rejoicing
with so many) I felt depressed, languid, and tired.
My appetite left me, and I was much distressed
after eating the lightest food. My skin and eyes
became tinged with a dark yellow colour, and the
kidney secretion was like blood. The pain in my
stomach was almost unbearable and often lasted
from 12 to 14 hours without intermission. Some-
times I was in pain night and day, and was so bad
that my wife had to sit up with me through the
night. I was constantly sick, and troubled with |
a stomach cough, and expectorated a quantity of
green phlegm.
“In spite of warm clothing and every comfort, j
I was always chilled; the cold shivers running
through me as if my blood were thin and poor. 1
could take no solid food ; I lived on soups, milk,
puddings, &c., and after each meal I had empty,
gnawing, windy pains at the stomach, which
nothing relieved.
“ After a time an intolerable itching of the skin
spread all over me, as if my blood were poisoned.
Our family physician attended me for about a
year. Acting upon his advice I went to Harro-
gate, where I consulted another doctor, and drank
the waters, but feeling worse I returned home.
The bath attendant at Harrogate and others told
me I was suffering from blood poisoning, but this
the doctors never mentioned. The first doctor
said it was the passing of gallstones that gave me
such dreadful pain.
“ I now consulted an eminent specialist at Man-
chester, who confirmed what the other doctor had
told me, but none of them afforded me any relief.
“In this miserable way I dragged on for six
months more, and became so much reduced I
could scarcely put one foot before the other, and
so thin that the rings fell off my fingers and rolled
on the floor. I was in such pain that I prayed to
die, and one of the doctors told a friend of mine I
could not recover.
“In August of last year (1890), whilst my suffer-
ings were at the worst, a book was sent to mo by
post, telling of a medicine called Mother SeigeLs
Curative Syrup. I determined to try it, and sent
to Mr. Evans, Chemist, Lymm,- for a supply.
After taking the first bottle, I felt a little better,
and by persevering with the remedy I recovered
my appetite, and gradually gained strength. My
natural colour is now returned, and I feel as well
as I ever did in my life ; in fact, as well as I did
when a boy. I can eat any kind of food without
inconvenience, and have gained 30 pounds in
weight during the past three months. I may add
that previous to taking this medicine I was so
much altered that my friends, and even my pupil?,
scarcely recognised me. I tell every one what
Seigel’s Syrup did for me. ”
The gentleman who makes the foregoing state-
ment is a person of position and known to all the
people of Lymm. He declines to permit the publi-
cation of his name, but the perfect truth of what
is here related is vouched for by Mr. J. H. Evans,
the chemist above named. _ , •
The case was an aggravated one of indigestion
and dyspepsia and its natural consequence. The
whole system had been poisoned and disordered by
the acids engendered by the fermentation in the
stomach, and, had not Seigel s Syrup come to the
rescue just as it did a fatal result must have
followed in a brief time.


MERELY THE TRUTH.
They have merit.
The fact is indisputable.
The proof lies in the free samples.
It is not enough to say they sometimes fail.
They are of human produce, and therefore fallible.
The real facts are—
Do they cure diseases of the blood and skin in the majority of in-
stances? Undoubtedly they do.
Do they undo the effect of a chill or congestion, by bringing about
normal conditions of health ? Yes, they loosen and disperse the congestion.
Do they permeate the blood aseptically, so that bacilli and germs
cannot multiply and the growth of disease is checked ? Yes, they do.
Do they confer relief, assuage pain, and benefit the general health,
even when they do not cure ? Invariably, yes.
Do they act as a natural healthy laxative, rendering them especially
valuable to women and children, and to men of sedentary habits of life or
business ? Yes, by tonic action only.
Do they free the inner skin pores and intricacies from taint, clearing
off eruptions and improving the complexion ? They do.
Does their palatable taste and safety render them pre-eminently a
family medicine? Yes, for neither the mind nor stomach rebels against
them, and children like them.
Are they the best of all blood purifiers and the most valuable of all
spring cleansing medicines ? Yes, they are.

Baby Miguel.
11 You kuo\V,” said Manuel, “ \Vhat a sotrowfiil
day for Tarragona was the 28th df June, 1811.
But ydu cattnot imagine the horrdrs attending
the taking Of the city. You did not see five
thousand Spaniards perish in ten hours ; yml did
tidt see houses and churches in flames ; you did
hot see miatmed old irteil Und helpless woolen
Slaughtered in cold blood ; you did not see the
ttlodesty Of maiderts, the dighity Of matrons, the
Sahctity Of nulls outraged. You dirt nOt see
jlillage and drunkenness mingling with lust and
Ihurdef. You did not see, in short, one of the
greatest exploits Of the conqueror of the world,
the herd Of oilr age, the demi-god Napoleon !
“1 saw it all! I saw tile sick rise from the bed
of suffering, dragging after them theit sheets, like
sHrduds, to perish at the hands of foreign soldiers
oh the threshold over which the day before had
passed the Viaticiirti! I saw lying in the street
the body of a woman they Had slaughtered, and
at her side her infant still nursing at its dead
jhothet’s breast. I saw the husband, with hands
tied together, witnessing the dishonor of his wife;
cliildreii weeping with tetror at the horrors that
surrounded them ; despair and innocence taking
refuge in suicide ; impiety insulting the dead.
“Dangerously wounded and unable to take
further part in the conflict, I fled for refuge to
Clara’s house.
“Full of anguish and terror, she stood at the
window fearing for my life and risking her own to
see me in case I should chance to through the
street.
“ I entered and fastened the door, but my
pursuers had already caught sight of Clara —and
she was so beautiful !
“ They saluted her with a roar of savage joy and
a burst of brutal laughter. A moment more and
the door would yield to the axe and the flames.
We were lost !
“Clara’s mother, carrying in her arms her year-
old babe, led us to the cistern or reservoir of the
house, which was very deep and which was now
dry, as no rain had fallen for several months, and
there we concealed ourselves. This cistern, the
floor of which might measure some eight yards’
square, and which was entered by a steep under-
ground flight of steps, narrowed, toward the top,
like the mouth of a well, and opened into the
centre of the courtyard, where a breast-work was
built around it, above which two buckets were
suspende i from hooks attached to an iron bar for
drawing water.
“The child I have spoken of, whose name was
Miguel, was Clara’s brother, that is to say, the
youngest child of the unhappy woman whom the
French had just made a widow.
“In the cistern we four might find safety.
Seen from the yard, the cistern seemed a little
well. The French would think we had made our
escape by the roof.
“ They soon declared that such was the case,
uttering horrible oaths while they rested them-
selves in the shady yard, in the centre of which
was the cistern.
“ Yes, we were saved ! Clara bound up my
bleeding hand, her mother nursed Miguel, and 1,
although I was shivering from the chill which
had followed the fever caused by my wound,
smiled with happiness.
“ At this moment, we noticed that the soldiers,
wishing, doubtless, to slake the r thirst, were
trying to draw water from the cistern in which we
were concealed.
“ Picture to yourselves our anguish at that
instant!
“ We drew aside to make way for the bucket,
which descended until it touched the floor.
“ We scarcely dared to breathe.
“ The bucket was drawn up again.
“ ‘The well is dry ! ’ cried the soldiers.
“‘There must be water upstairs !’ exclaimed
one.
“ ‘They are go ng aWay !* we all said to our
selves.
“‘What if th-y should bo c moc.aled ill this
well ! cried a voice iu Catalan.
“It was a i'enegadc !—a Spaniard who had be-
trayed us !
“ ‘ What nonsense ! ’ responded the Frenchman;
* they could not have got down there so suddenly !’
“ ‘ That is true,’ responded the renegade.
“ They did not know that the cistern could be
entered by an underground passage, whose door
or trap, carefully concealed in the floor of a dark
cellar, somewhat distant from the house, it would
be almost impossible to discover. Wo had, how-
ever, committed the imprudence of locking the
iron grating which cut oflf the communication
between the cistern and the passage, and we could
not open it without making a great deal of noise
“ Imagine, then, the cruel fluctuations between
hope and tear with which we had listened to the
dialogue carried on by those, wretches on the very
brink of the well. From the corners in which we
were crouching wo could see the shadow of their
heads moving within the circle of light on the
floor of the cistern. Every second seemed to us a
century.
“At this moment the babe Miguel began to cry.
“But at his first whimper his mother silenced
the sounds that threatened to betray our hiding-
place, pressing the infant’s tender face into her
bosom.
“‘Did you hear that?’ cried someone in the
yard above.
“ ‘ I heard nothing,’ responded another-.
“ * Let us listen,’ said the renegade.
“Three horrible minutes passed.
“ Miguel struggled to get his voice, and the
more closely his mother pressed his face into her
bosom, the more violent Were bis struggles.
“ But not the slightest sound was audible.
“‘lt must have been an echo !’ exclaimed the
soldiers.
“ ‘ Yes, that was it!’ assented the renegade.
“And t-cy all took their departure, and we
could hear the noise of their steps and the clanking
of their sabres slowly dying away in the direction
of the gate.
“The danger was passed !
“ But, alas ! our deliverance had come too late.
“ Baby Miguel neither cried nor struggled now.
“ lie was deqxl!”
ARGONAUT Trunslation.
Tile Spanish papers tell of tile deatli of a
priest named Laforga—the Stratigest and wildest
fathet who ever liVed ill the Pehitlsula. He WaS
a brilliaiit preacher, and tail half-a-dozen brpllall-
flges at his own expense. J'lvety waif and stray
bf the city he Was willing to take in and provide
for ; he found food and work for almost everybody
Ivho was dead broke and hungry ; lie read the
burial-service over suicides, heretics, and dead
ladies of easy virtue Whom no other priest Would
bury, and “ shouted ” the tombstone if necessary ;
and wherever there was pestilence or any other
trouble he rolled up. When money ran short for
these works of charity he went out to nocturnal
dives, and gambled and drank furiously, and
whether the holy man stacked the cards or not he
made it fearfully hot for the ungodly. His chief
recreation was haunting the back regions of the
theatre, and hugging the ballet-girls; he also
attended bull-fights and fought sundry duels. The
Church dropped on him heavily, but he told it to
go to perdition, and his congregation raised such
a dust on his behalf that he always got through.
He did more good, and saw more dubious girls
home late at night, and won more money at cards,
and painted Madrid a brighter red than any other
priest on record ; and when he died all the poor
and the hungry, and most of the bull-fighters,
gamblers, and ladies who wore tights and had
several husbands came and wept bitterly over his
dust.

The New York Sun tells tile latest stbry
df the liystetical and Irresponsible fenidle the one
who gets up the Momlt Rennie otitl-age story,
aild tells Sensational roiliancea about inen with
blackeried faces who have waylaid het, and cut off
Her hair, and otherwise distinghishes herself. In
the present instance the girl’s trohble was caused
by anonymous letters. They cattle in shoals to
her and to her friends —letters iri feigned hand-
writings, in which some base deceiver maligned
her character and cast all manner of foul asper-
sions at her. The detectives toiled in vain to get
on the track of the miscreant who had done it,
and while they were toiling the girl faded away
with worry and anxiety and grief; and died.
Her last hours were full of pathos; she
lay on a little white bed, with her weep-
ing friends around her, and forgave her un-
known enemy, and then her voice grew fainter
and her lungs gave out, and she passed away.
Afterwards her bereaved father overhauled her
desk, and found the original rough drafts of the
letters, together with some notes from another
hysterical girl of the same order in which she—the
other hysterical girl—promised to copy them out
for her, and post tin in. The deceased had written
them all herself, and 1 1 ad died through living up
to the character of the persecuted saint; in other
words, she had tried to pine away a little, and to
fade somewhat, so as to keep up the part, and she
had inadvertently fadtd too much and peri lied
through it. Her heart was broken on account of
the aspersions she cast on her own character, but
even in her last moments she kept the circus
going, and her end was put in a Sunday-.'chool
book for the benefit of the young before she was
found out.
